Key moments
Profile
Why this call is the most unusual in the dataset
Unique
First churn-back win — former RepeatMD customer returning for Ageless
Dusan was a RepeatMD customer at least a year ago and churned because the Canadian payment processing didn't support Affirm financing — a real limitation at the time that prevented patients from purchasing through the app. He stayed away until Instagram ads for Ageless caught his attention. Mike handled the return factually: acknowledged the past limitation, confirmed Canadian payment processing relaunched in June 2024, and pivoted cleanly to Ageless as a separate product. Dusan responded: "It wasn't like a service issue. It was just, we couldn't convert as much and get those sales right away. So, like I was saying... I'm sold. What do we, what's the cost?"

This is the first documented case of a churned RepeatMD customer returning as an Ageless close. It validates a real pipeline — former customers who left for reasons that have since been resolved can be actively re-engaged, and Ageless is a lower-friction entry product than trying to convince them to re-adopt RepeatMD directly.
Document as a named playbook: "Churn-back motion." The sequence that worked: (1) acknowledge the past limitation without defensiveness, (2) state specifically what has changed since (with dates), (3) pivot to Ageless as a separate product rather than trying to re-pitch RepeatMD, (4) once Ageless is live, the upsell path back to RepeatMD is natural. Every former RepeatMD customer who churned pre-June 2024 for payment reasons is now a qualified prospect — worth a list pull.
Unique
Thursday webinar — hardest deadline in the dataset
Dusan has a webinar on April 23 (three days after this call) and wants the Ageless QR code live for it so attendees can try it on their own faces during the presentation. This created real urgency that Mike and Zack converted into an on-call commitment: Mike slacked the CTO, founder Phil, and Matt mid-call to pull Dusan off the waitlist same-night. The deal closed during that slack exchange.

This is the hardest event-driven deadline the War Room has seen. Wenzel had a mid-June LA launch; NaDene was pre-launch pending a website; Stephanie was two weeks out from a site relaunch. Dusan was three days from a live event. That compression forced the team to escalate internally in real-time and ship a non-standard onboarding flow to get him deployed.
When a prospect surfaces a live-event deadline within a week, treat it as a deal accelerator, not an objection. The sequence: (1) confirm the event date and what Ageless needs to do (QR code for live demo, custom tracking link, etc.), (2) commit to an expedited timeline on the call — not "we'll try" but "I'll slack the CTO right now and get confirmation," (3) use the urgency as the anchor for card-on-call. The pattern works because hard deadlines align prospect motivation with Ageless's ability to deliver fast, and it bypasses the usual "I'll think about it" stall.
Unique
Creator-physician hybrid with 26K engaged Instagram followers
Dusan's organic social is his primary acquisition channel and it's working — 26K followers with 10K+ views per post, 100+ likes, 300-400 comments per post, all organic. Paid Meta ads have failed across three different agencies including Chase Chappell's team. Google paid ads work for the clinic but not for skincare. He is, functionally, an influencer who happens to be a physician, and his growth strategy is doubling down on personal brand rather than paid acquisition.

This is a completely different acquisition profile from any other prospect in the dataset. For Dusan, Ageless lives on Instagram Stories and link-in-bio, not a Meta ad. Zack reframed the tool correctly mid-call: "you can leverage your social following — do stories, do reels, put it in link-in-bio, and drive traffic organically from social. Those are going to be more qualified to begin with." This is the right pitch for creator-physicians and it's not documented anywhere else in the tool.
Add "creator-physician" as a named prospect type alongside the existing categories. The pitch mechanics are different: (a) lead with Instagram/social deployment, not website deployment, (b) skip Meta ads entirely — they don't work for this profile, (c) the link-in-bio + Stories rotation is the primary deployment, (d) white-labeling is critical because they're building their brand, not Ageless's. Dusan's call should be the teaching example for this type.
What worked
Strong
Zack's best solo discovery in the dataset
Before Mike joined, Zack asked a tight four-question sequence: (1) what are you currently doing for lead gen, (2) what's working and not working on paid ads, (3) who handles lead follow-up, (4) what CRM are you using. Each answer shaped the next question without a template. Dusan described 26K-follower organic social as the primary channel, Meta ads failing across multiple agencies, Google ads working for clinic, skincare needing a separate strategy, and Pabau as the CRM. Zack's discovery gave Mike enough context to join mid-call and ask sharper follow-ups — the handoff worked because the discovery did.
This is the strongest example of solo AE discovery in the War Room. Worth using as a coaching example specifically for discovery sequencing: each question narrowed the next one, and none of them were scripted. Zack asked about his acquisition stack in the order Dusan answered it, which is the cleanest way to do non-templated discovery.
Strong
Mike's "don't chase Meta" read saved an hour of wrong-direction pitching
Early in the call, Dusan mentioned trying Meta ads with three different agencies and failing. Mike asked what "good" and "not good" looked like, then diagnosed: Dusan has 26K followers and an established personal brand — he doesn't need paid Meta because he can leverage social directly. Mike then laid out the acquisition reframe: Instagram Stories + link-in-bio + organic posts is the correct Ageless deployment for Dusan, not paid ads. This read re-oriented the rest of the call around Dusan's actual acquisition strengths rather than trying to retrofit Ageless into a paid-ad motion that had failed.
When a prospect has tried paid acquisition multiple times and it hasn't worked, don't pitch it again. Ask what has worked and build the Ageless deployment around that channel. For creator-physicians specifically, organic social is the answer — Meta ads against an organic-first follower base actively damage the algorithm's read on the account.
Gaps
Gap
LA launch timing surfaced late — should have been opening question
The fact that Dusan is opening an LA clinic "next week" surfaced at 16:00 on a 55-minute call. Mike immediately clocked the missed opportunity: "I wish we could have got you a month or two ago to help you out." A full month of pre-launch Ageless deployment would have built a waitlist before the doors opened. Instead, Ageless is going live within days of the actual launch.
Add "any new locations, launches, or events in the next 6 months?" to standard discovery. Getting this information in the first two minutes of the call allows the rep to position Ageless specifically for that launch and creates much better economics for the prospect. For Dusan, this question answered late still closed the deal but left significant pre-launch value on the table.
Well handled
"We worked with you guys in the past — it didn't convert as much"
Dusan raised his churn history in the first three minutes. Mike handled it factually without defensiveness: acknowledged the Canadian payment processing limitation was real, confirmed it relaunched in June 2024, and pivoted cleanly to Ageless as a separate product. He didn't try to re-pitch RepeatMD, didn't apologize excessively, and didn't explain the history in more detail than necessary. Dusan accepted the framing and moved on.
This is the textbook churn-back objection handling. The formula: (1) acknowledge the past issue factually, (2) state what changed and when, (3) pivot to the current product conversation without re-litigating the old one. Never defend, never over-explain, never re-pitch the churned product until the new one is closed. Mike did all three in under 30 seconds.
Well handled
"Can I monetize this — charge patients $10 per visualization?"
Dusan asked specifically about gating visualizations behind a $10 payment, reasoning that wives would pay to show husbands their future self. Mike reframed with "Penny wise, pound foolish" — if you gate the experience, you get way fewer people engaging. The point of Ageless isn't the image sale; it's the follow-up data, the scored lead, the emotional commitment that converts to treatment. A botox patient becomes a botox + filler + microneedling + CoolSculpting patient worth far more than $10 per image. Dusan accepted it with "Okay. Fair."
Expect this objection from creator-physicians who think transactionally about their audience. The answer has to reframe Ageless from "tool that produces images" to "funnel that produces lifetime-value patients." The image isn't the product — the patient relationship that starts with the image is the product.
Well handled
"Is there a way to white-label this? Call it Sai-itch AI?"
Dusan asked whether he could rebrand the tool entirely as his product. Mike confirmed yes — logo, color, name branding are all customizable. This is important because Dusan is building a consumer brand ("Sai-itch Skin Longevity") and the tool needs to look like his own product, not like someone else's service. The white-label confirmation was the moment Dusan committed to the close.
For creator-physicians specifically, white-labeling is not a nice-to-have — it's a deal-breaker. They're building audiences and brands that compete on proprietary-feeling products. Any hint that the tool looks like Ageless instead of their brand weakens their positioning. Lead with the white-label capability in the pitch rather than making it a follow-up answer.
Effective
Real-time CTO escalation converted hard deadline into a close
When Dusan mentioned the Thursday webinar, Mike opened Slack mid-call and messaged Phil, JT, and Matt to confirm he could be expedited off the waitlist that night. This was a live, visible escalation that Dusan could see happening — not a "I'll check and get back to you" stall. Within about 10 minutes, CTO confirmation came back and Mike said "I just confirmed with our CTO that we can scooch you right off the waitlist so we can get you ready." The card was entered shortly after.
Real-time internal escalation is a high-leverage closing tool when a prospect has a hard deadline. Document the play: (1) prospect surfaces event-driven urgency, (2) rep opens Slack/messaging in parallel with the conversation, (3) rep frames the escalation as a commitment signal ("let me confirm with the CTO right now"), (4) return from the escalation with a specific answer, not a maybe. The visible escalation itself is part of the close — the prospect sees that Ageless is willing to move mountains for them, which triggers reciprocity.
Effective
Dropped call recovery — Zack held the line, Mike kept framing
Dusan's connection dropped twice — once at 14:22 (his spotty internet) and once at 30:00 (phone battery died). Each time, Zack stayed composed, texted him, and reconnected cleanly. During the second dropped call segment, Mike used the downtime to Slack internal stakeholders about expediting the deal, so when Dusan rejoined Mike could say "I just confirmed with our CTO." This turned a technical failure into an acceleration moment rather than a momentum-killer.
Composure during technical failures is a named rep capability that Dominique demonstrated in the Jamey payment fiasco and Zack demonstrated here. The play: (1) never express frustration or stress, (2) use the interruption productively (Slack escalation, parallel work), (3) reconnect with a forward-motion statement rather than a "sorry about that" apology, (4) treat the interruption as the prospect's context, not a failure of the call. Both reps did this well and both deals closed.
Watch
Skincare visualization ask — real product gap surfaced
At the end of the call, Dusan asked directly: "Is there any appetite on working on a skincare version of this that you want to pilot and develop?" He has validated third-party testing (PAOT test, mitochondrial tests), high-resolution before-and-afters from a Visia imaging system, and an Eve Lab setup. He's offering to contribute data and co-develop a skincare-specific visualization module. Mike said "I'll bring it up" and acknowledged the idea is neat. This is a real product opportunity that should get escalated — a skincare visualization layer would open a distinct prospect category (skincare-only practices, product lines, compounding pharmacies) that Ageless currently cannot serve.
Flag to Phil and product team: Dusan has offered to provide training data and clinical validation for a skincare visualization module. This is a volunteer beta user with a credible scientific background (PAOT validation, Visia imaging system, his own brand) who would be the ideal development partner. Worth a direct conversation — even if skincare visualization isn't on the roadmap, capturing his data and his offer to help keeps the option open. Don't lose him as a potential product partner.

Dusan is the most unusual prospect in the dataset on multiple axes simultaneously: a former churned RepeatMD customer, a creator-physician with an established personal brand, and a practice owner opening a new LA location within a week of the call. The combination is specific enough that it will be rare, but the individual patterns (churn-back, creator-physician, hard-deadline event) are each repeatable motions worth documenting.

The deal closed because of three things happening in parallel: (1) Mike's clean handling of the churn-back objection without defensiveness, (2) real-time CTO escalation that converted the Thursday deadline into an on-call close, (3) white-label confirmation that aligned Ageless with Dusan's brand-building strategy. Any one of those moments fails and the deal doesn't close on this call.

Account management priorities:
(1) Deploy for the Thursday webinar — QR code, custom tracking link, white-labeled as Sai-itch AI. (2) LA launch support — the link needs to be ready before the clinic opens, Instagram Stories deployment planned before the doors open. (3) Skincare visualization conversation — escalate Dusan's offer to be a development partner to Phil directly. (4) Peptides visualization — he'll want BPC-157 and Tesamorelin specifically, confirm these are in the training data or add them.

Long-term flag:
Dusan is a potential RepeatMD re-adoption prospect 3-6 months after Ageless goes live. The payment processing limitation that caused his original churn is resolved, and once Ageless is generating the volume he's expecting, the retention layer becomes valuable again. Don't pitch RepeatMD now — pitch it once he has data showing Ageless is working.

Exceptional — 9/10
Mike surfaced the Ontario regulatory shift and the lifestyle-medicine pivot — deepest context in the dataset
At 8:05, Jen volunteered a major regulatory detail: Ontario's federal government just announced they're removing the NP private-medicine loophole. Her practice is shifting entire focus to lifestyle medicine — naturopaths + MD integrated care + peptides + weight management. Mike's follow-up questions ("what's the lifestyle medicine?" "does that include GLP-1s and HRT?") produced a full operational picture: physician trained in lifestyle medicine for 10+ years, chelation therapy, ozone therapy, naturopath handles autoimmune/inflammation/hormone balancing, integrated MD-MD consultation. Patients have third-party benefits. This is the most context-dense discovery segment in the War Room. 9/10 reflects the depth of the operational picture surfaced.
For Canadian prospects specifically, regulatory context shifts quickly and reps should ask "has anything changed in the regulatory environment that's affecting how you're structuring the practice?" This open-ended question surfaced Jen's entire pivot in one answer. Worth adding to Canadian-prospect discovery as a standard question. For any regulated profession, the regulatory-environment question is a high-value discovery signal.
Strong
VISIA already in-practice — Mike positioned Ageless as the phone-based upstream layer
Jen opened by saying "right now we have the vizia face analysis, which is great, but if this is something the client can be doing, is it like before they even come into the office?" She identified the gap herself — VISIA is in-practice only. Mike's clean framing: "VISIA is like really more skin focused and Ageless should cover your full practice menu including like your wellness treatments." Jen accepted immediately. VISIA complementary positioning worked as the updated Battle Card specifies.
When prospects have VISIA and articulate the upstream-phone-based gap themselves, you don't need to convince them — confirm and move to demo. The VISIA battle card framing ("VISIA closes in the chair, Ageless fills the chair") continues to convert.
Strong
Saturday event anchor surfaced at close — 10 miles from Buffalo, wants cross-border radius
Late in the call Jen surfaced that she's 10 miles from Buffalo and wants to advertise across the border. This is a specific Canadian-US dynamic worth flagging: practices in border cities have legitimate cross-border patient acquisition needs. Mike confirmed the radius is configurable and committed to including Buffalo. Combined with the Saturday event deadline, the close moved to "get her live before Saturday." JT CTO got pulled in via Slack for expedited onboarding.
For Canadian border-city practices, ask specifically about cross-border patient flow. US-side patients crossing to Canada for care is a real pattern (exchange rate, wait times, specific service availability). The radius configuration needs to accommodate this. Worth flagging to onboarding for Canadian-border accounts.
Direct question
"Have you worked out all the glitches already?" — beta tester mindset
At 47:13 Jen asked point-blank: "I'm pretty tech savvy and I have a medical background... we've been beta testers for several platforms. Have you worked out all the glitches already?" This is the sophisticated-prospect version of the product-maturity objection. Mike's answer was direct: "we've had beta clients using Ageless for close to six months. The kinks are worked out. We picked the hardball people — the high-profile repeatmd clients and a lot of the advisory board. So we stress-tested it with very discerning people." This landed cleanly. Jen's next line was "Okay, you know what? Let's do it."
For technically sophisticated prospects who have been beta testers for other platforms, the "we've had beta customers for 6 months and stress-tested with discerning users" framing is the right answer. Don't claim perfection — claim rigorous testing with hard users. Mike's phrasing is reusable: "we picked the hardball people" signals confidence without overpromising.
Configuration question
"Can we set the geographic radius to include Buffalo?"
Jen wants to advertise across the US-Canada border (10mi from Buffalo). Mike confirmed the radius is configurable but couldn't show the exact UI during the call — "I think it's in the initial setup, not seeing it in settings." Jen accepted the commitment. This wasn't a deal-blocker but was a configuration gap worth flagging: the radius setting's location in the UI should be clearer.
Flag to product: the geographic radius setting location is unclear. Mike had to search for it live and couldn't find it. For border-market accounts (Canadian practices near US, Mexican border cities, etc.), this setting is operationally critical. Worth making it visible in the primary settings panel, not buried in initial setup only.
Effective
JT CTO expedite for Saturday deployment — internal escalation activated
When Jen said "Saturday would be lovely," Mike Slacked JT (the CTO) directly during the call to expedite onboarding. "I'm gonna Slack JT and boom, it's like our CTO and the head programmer over there. I'll just use them, cool." This is the operational VP-flex move — Mike had the internal relationships to compress a 48-72 hour normal deployment to Saturday (2 days). Jen was visibly appreciated: "Yes, that would be lovely."
Executive-level deployment expedites are a real close aid for event-anchored deals. The ability to Slack the CTO mid-call and commit to an accelerated timeline signals that Mike has the org relationships to make things happen — which is what a prospect like Jen (who has beta-tested products before and dealt with slow engineering teams) is implicitly asking about with the "glitches worked out" question. Worth noting as a pattern: for time-sensitive close calls with sophisticated buyers, surfacing internal-execution-access can be the difference between "let me think about it" and "let's do it."
Effective
Canadian pricing clarification — Alex caught the exchange rate confusion live
During pricing walkthrough, Alex flagged: "Jen, know that all the pricing that we're mentioning is in Canadian dollars. So you don't have to make that conversion over to the U.S." Jen laughed: "Oh my god, that's where I was like, oh no." Small detail, big moment — catching the currency confusion before it became a post-close objection. Clean operational discipline for Canadian prospects.
For all Canadian prospects, confirm pricing is in CAD explicitly early in the pricing walkthrough. The difference between "499 USD" and "499 CAD" is roughly 35% — that's significant enough to kill a deal post-signature if the prospect assumed USD. Alex's catch here should be standard for all Canadian AE calls.

Key moments
Profile
What worked
Strong
Jennifer asked where the disconnect is — not just what the channels are
After learning Mari uses SEO, PPC, and Facebook, Jennifer asked: "Where do you feel like the biggest disconnect is — ad to booking, booking to showing up, or showing up to converting?" This is a more sophisticated discovery question than any asked across the other six calls. It maps the specific conversion leak rather than just the traffic source.
"I think that is more of a problem of social media ads... with Google ads, you're just capturing people that are ready to do the treatment."
This question should be in every rep's standard discovery sequence. It forces the prospect to self-identify exactly where they're losing, which tells you which Ageless capability to lead with.
Strong
Mari arrived already having built a prototype — met at product level
Mari had already built a paper questionnaire that evolved to a digital iPad questionnaire that populates treatment recommendations based on patient conditions. She knew exactly what she was trying to build. Matt and Jennifer correctly recognized this and met her at product level rather than pitching from scratch.
"This is exactly what we've been trying to prototype... if we have this probably ready at some point, this can be an ad as well."
When a prospect has already tried to build what you sell, your job is to show them why your version is better, not to explain why the concept matters. Jennifer did this well by letting Mari's own language lead the conversation.
Strong
Schedule capacity and new provider hiring surfaced naturally
Jennifer asked "Are you at a place where we're really looking for new patients?" — a direct, unafraid question. Mari revealed she recently hired a new provider she needs to get busy, and has plans to bring in an NMD for wellness and hormones. Both pieces of intel sharpened the pitch in real time.
This question deserves a place in every call's discovery sequence. It reveals urgency, capacity, and growth direction in one question.
Strong
Matt named the wellness pivot and its implications immediately
When Mari mentioned plans to bring in an NMD for wellness and hormones, Matt immediately said "there's a real effective cross-sell strategy we can deploy just for when it comes to introducing those patients to those newer services." Exact right connection, made immediately.
This is the wellness pipeline angle deployed correctly — connecting a stated future plan to a specific Ageless capability before the demo even starts.
Well handled
"Can that be linked to our own website / branded?"
Mari asked multiple times whether the Ageless experience would show her brand, live on her URL, and feel native to SkinLab. Jennifer consistently confirmed yes — unique links, her logo, goes everywhere. Clean and confident.
Well handled
"Can we use your system and our own CRM simultaneously?"
Mari asked whether manual follow-up would disrupt the automation sequence. Matt gave a clear three-option answer: Ageless automation, manual through the admin panel, or route to GHL. Then confirmed both can coexist. Excellent — no ambiguity left.
Well handled
"What happens if we send to our 10,000 patient database and they all sign up?"
Mari has 10,000+ patients and was concerned about what counting looks like at scale. Jennifer correctly clarified that patients only count when they complete the full flow and enter their information — not when an email is sent. Removed the pricing anxiety cleanly.
This is a recurring question from larger practices. Have the answer memorized: "Sending the link costs you nothing. A patient only counts when they upload their photo and enter their information — so you can blast your entire database and only pay for the ones who actually engage."
Effective
Friday follow-up with urgency anchor
Jennifer set a specific Friday call with a genuine urgency anchor — the AmSpa promotion expires end of day Friday and the Monday launch date is real. Mari committed to "call me before 2pm on Friday." Clean next step with a real deadline.
Deadline-based urgency works when the deadline is real. This one is. Don't let it slip into Monday — the deal energy drops fast over a weekend without a close.
Watch
Partner approval needed — not in the room
Mari mentioned wanting to share the recording with her partner. The partner is a decision-maker who wasn't on the call and hasn't seen the demo. Friday's close depends on someone who has only seen a recording.
Before Friday: send a tight summary email with the three things the partner most needs to see — the demo visualization, the pricing structure, and the testimonial numbers. Offer to do a 15-minute call with both of them Friday if the partner has questions. Don't let the partner's approval happen asynchronously through a recording.

Key moments
Profile
What worked
Strong
Mike dug into failed marketing experience — gold
Linda mentioned she'd tried marketing three times and found it unhelpful. Instead of moving on, Mike asked three follow-up questions: What kind of marketing? What agency? When you called them, what was the response like? This excavation revealed the core problem: promotion-anchored ads brought low-quality, price-sensitive patients who didn't rebook at full price.
"What's the promotion? Can I get it cheaper? They're not rebooking because they don't want to pay full price once they've had it at a lower ticketed price."
This is exactly how to handle a prospect who has tried and failed at marketing. Don't skip it — dig in. The failure story tells you everything about what they value and what they're afraid of repeating.
Strong
Mike reframed the lead quality problem before the demo
Before Dominique even started the demo, Mike gave a sharp 60-second explanation of why Linda's past marketing failed — the anchor was a discount, which creates a low-value lifetime customer. He then set up Ageless as curiosity-anchored rather than discount-anchored. This is the ideal sequence: solve the past problem in the framing, then show the solution.
"The anchor of ageless is curiosity. They want to learn what these treatments are going to be like... not 'can I get the botox for nine bucks a unit?'"
This pre-demo reframe is one of the best moments across all 6 calls. It should be a standard part of the playbook when a prospect has a bad prior marketing experience.
Strong
Live demo on Linda's actual face
Linda asked to try it on herself. Dominique had her text a selfie and a side profile mid-call, then ran her through the full flow live. Linda got an 81 beauty score, saw her own before-and-after (cheek filler, RF microneedling), and her Future Aging Self. The emotional reaction was immediate and genuine.
Running a live demo on the prospect's face is the single most powerful thing you can do on a call. Dominique found a way to do it even without a proper demo link. This should be a standard offer on every call: "Would you want to try it on yourself right now?"

Strong
Alex named the under-promise-over-deliver concern without flinching
Elliot's core objection came out early — he's deeply committed to setting realistic expectations and was worried about patients arriving with a visualization and demanding an exact match. Alex handled it by leading with advisory board credibility (Rajani, Nassif, Shino Bay), explaining the 15-month build process, and differentiating from ChatGPT-style consumer AI. Solid framing that respected the concern rather than dismissing it.
The answer got the credibility right but missed the strategic reframe that works best for cautious clinical prospects: the visualization is the expectation-setting conversation. Right now patients arrive with an undocumented mental image — with Ageless, you're sitting down together documenting what's achievable and what isn't, with you as the authority. That flip is what converts skeptics. Alex got there partially at 17:05 with "80-85 to 90 percent closer than you would without doing a damn thing" but the reframe never fully landed.

Well handled

When a prospect raises the same objection three times, the answer isn't landing. By the third return, the right move is to acknowledge the pattern: "I can see this is the core question for you. Context

This is the cleanest close in the dataset by time-to-card.

The 5/10 discovery score reflects what wasn't asked, not what went wrong. The call didn't need more discovery to close — but the absence of it means the account is going to hit implementation without a clear picture of services, acquisition channels, or the role structure between Dwayne, Kelly, and Scott.
What worked
Strong
Alex confirmed nobody else was joining before pitching — efficient room check
At 4:45 Alex asked "Is anyone else joining this call today or is just us?" and got a clean answer (Kelly is with Collin, Scott not on). This is the decision-maker check the Discovery tab calls for, asked at exactly the right moment. Dwayne confirmed he was authorized to move forward, which cleared the close.
Correct application of the decision-maker check question. Worth noting the structural difference from Mari and Rebekah: in both of those calls the decision-maker was absent and the deal stalled. Here the decision-maker was present, the team had pre-aligned, and it closed in 18 minutes. This is the deal shape you want — and the question confirms you have it.
Strong
Branded asset walkthrough matched Dwayne's specific questions
Dwayne asked three variations of "is it branded to us" early in the call. Alex's first response was to scroll through the admin panel showing the exact customization (logo, colors, font control). This is the right posture — Dwayne was asking an operational question, not an objection, and Alex gave him operational evidence rather than a positioning pitch.
For pre-sold prospects, the close shifts from persuasion to demonstration. Alex did this well. The next-iteration tweak: when a prospect asks three variations of the same question, skip to "here's exactly what your version will look like" and show the specific admin customization fields — that closes the question in one beat instead of three.
Gaps
Gap
No discovery on services, volume, or acquisition channels — implementation risk
Alex closed without learning what The Powder Room offers (injectables? lasers? wellness?), what their monthly patient volume looks like, what they currently use for acquisition, or what their EMR situation is. Dwayne did mention 9,000 past clients and 5,000 followers unprompted, which is useful — but it came up in passing, not as part of a structured discovery conversation.

The bigger gap: Dwayne mentioned they're "bringing in all kinds of equipment, new stuff" and "it's probably going to take us three or four months to get everything down." This is a practice mid-transition, which is exactly the profile where activation risk is highest — they may sign up today and not actually deploy the link for months.
When a prospect is pre-sold and the close is moving fast, a 3-question discovery micro-sequence still helps: (1) What services are you leading with right now? (2) How many patients a month are you seeing? (3) Where does most of your current marketing point? 90 seconds, shapes the onboarding, surfaces the real priorities. Worth running even on easy closes.
Gap
RMD-paused status never explored — structural signal about how they'll use Ageless

The reason for the pause, what would reactivate it, and whether Ageless alone will meet their acquisition needs without the RMD retention layer — all unexplored. This matters because Ageless's signature move for existing customers is pairing acquisition with retention, and Dwayne is running half the stack.
Short, targeted question worth asking any RMD-paused prospect: "What would need to be true for you to unpause RepeatMD alongside this?" Gives you the account's actual needs and flags whether they're a candidate for the combined retention/acquisition play once Ageless proves out.
Well handled
"What if she doesn't look like that?" (Scott's question by proxy)
Dwayne raised the expectation-setting concern on behalf of his partner Scott. Alex handled it in the same pattern that's working across the dataset: advisory board credibility, clinically accurate training, 15-month build process, disclaimer language, "you'll get much closer than doing nothing." Dwayne accepted it immediately — "no right, oh I get it, I was just, yeah, that was just his question." Clean handling of a proxied objection.
The pattern to note here: when an objection is being relayed ("Scott asked" / "my wife wanted to know"), the standard is lower than when it's the person's own concern — they're transmitting, not holding. Alex read this correctly and didn't over-answer. This matters because proxied objections can trick reps into a long explanation the prospect doesn't actually need.
Well handled
"No long-term contract?" — double-checked against the RMD 90-day window
Dwayne asked if Ageless had a contract, then specifically checked whether Ageless inherited RMD's 90-day opt-out. The distinction matters because Dwayne's RMD experience had made him contract-cautious, and the stronger Ageless terms removed the last hesitation.
For any existing or former RMD customer, proactively flagging "Ageless is stronger on cancellation terms than RepeatMD — true month-to-month, cancel in-product" before they ask is worth doing. Dwayne's question signals this is a real concern in the installed base.
Note
Not an objection — but a window into the RMD experience

This is a real RMD operator complaint about reward structure, and Dwayne had already had this same conversation with his consultants (Lisa et al.). This isn't an Ageless objection but it's adjacent intelligence worth capturing: the RMD installed base has a rewards-generosity perception problem that surfaces even in Ageless calls. The Patterns tab should track this as an adjacent signal.
Effective

When a prospect is verbally closed, the next two minutes matter more than the previous twenty. Keep the momentum.
Effective

Flag this account for proactive check-in at day 14, day 30, and day 60 — not "how's it going?" check-ins but "here's a specific thing to try this week" check-ins. The founders WhatsApp helps but doesn't substitute for a direct touchpoint during the transition window.

What worked
Strong
Fozia surfaced the core problem herself in the first two minutes
Zack asked about marketing. Fozia immediately named the issue without prompting: Canadian economy has hit disposable income, Meta ads used to bring quality leads for $8/day and have degraded substantially, and she's now looking for a tool that brings fewer but higher-quality patients. She explicitly said "maybe there'll be five, but like, you know, more of a higher quality." This is the exact framing Ageless was built for.
When a prospect diagnoses their own problem in the opening exchange, the job shifts from discovery to confirmation. Zack handled this well — he validated the problem ("if you can get five good patients that are kind of up the right alley, that's worth a lot more than 500 people who just click a link") and pivoted directly to product mechanics without over-discovering.
Strong
"Click happy people" — captured vocabulary worth reusing
Fozia described her low-quality leads as "click happy people — just sitting there scrolling at night, clicking, not really interested." This is a vivid, reusable prospect phrase that names the pain precisely. Worth capturing as a vocabulary hook for future discovery — when a rep asks about lead quality, if the prospect uses a version of this phrase, it signals a near-lock prospect for Ageless's scoring system.
Add to the vocabulary bank: "Click happy people" = Meta ad tire-kickers who never convert. When you hear it, lead with the scoring/enrichment story rather than the visualization story — they've already diagnosed the lead quality problem, they just need the solution.
Strong
Zack asked about the lead process before pitching
"If somebody clicks a meta ad right now, what happens then — like does your team follow up?" Fozia answered: "within five seconds, ten seconds — within three minutes for sure." Zack correctly identified that a fast-follow-up team still losing to lead quality means the problem is upstream, not downstream. This sets up Ageless as an acquisition tool, not a follow-up tool — which changes the demo emphasis.
Ask this question on every call regardless of what the prospect says about their problem. Speed-to-lead reveals whether the issue is acquisition quality or follow-up execution, and the answer determines which half of Ageless's value prop to lead with.
Gaps
Gap
Multi-location situation surfaced late — not leveraged
At 35:00, Fozia mentioned almost in passing that they have two locations (the original Retreat Skin & Body and the 3D Lifestyle franchise they absorbed). Zack acknowledged it and moved on. Multi-location practices are a distinct Ageless use case — the same link deploys to both locations, patient data enriches the full database, and the acquisition pipeline serves both sites. This should have been a 5-minute conversation, not a 30-second mention at the end.
Build multi-location into standard early discovery: "How many locations do you operate?" — ideally as one of the first three questions. It changes the pitch (enterprise scale), the pricing conversation (volume allocation), and opens the acquisition/retention both-sides story. Flag for Aria on the handoff call.
Well handled
"Somebody told me there was a free first month — is that still a thing?"
Fozia referenced a friend who had a demo four or five days earlier and mentioned a free first month. Zack handled this honestly — explained that was the original launch-day $1 trial from the growth summit webinar, and that it was pulled because it generated unqualified signups ("people signing up just weren't committed"). Then pivoted cleanly to the current founders club value: unlimited patients first month + WhatsApp community + skip the waitlist.
The $1 trial era is going to keep coming up for the next few weeks. Have a clean one-line answer ready: "That was the launch webinar offer — we pulled it because it brought in practices who weren't serious. The founders club replaces it with unlimited patients your first month at $499, which is a better deal if you actually plan to use the product."
Partial
"Is there another perk — maybe a trial for a week?"
Fozia asked directly if there was anything beyond the founders club. Zack held the line correctly — "to my knowledge, we're not doing anything additional outside of the founders club deal" — and reframed to the unlimited patients angle. The reframe worked but could have been sharper. He explained the reasoning ("we don't want you to spend $500 and feel like you lost money") which is good, but the stronger version connects it directly to her volume.
The sharper reframe: "You've got 4,000 patients in your database. If Aria blasts your Ageless link to even a quarter of them in the first two weeks, you'll hit fifty patients on a normal tier. The unlimited first month is structured exactly for this — it's not a trial in disguise, it's a way to run the entire database through without a cap. That's the test you actually want."
Effective
Clean BDR-to-AE handoff booked on the call — same day, 4:30pm
Zack identified the real decision-maker early (Aria, the clinic manager and consultant). Rather than try to close Fozia directly, he confirmed Fozia's approval authority ("she can sign off" — yes), then locked a specific time on the same call while Fozia physically walked to Aria's office to confirm. This is the textbook BDR-to-AE handoff — warm prospect, decision-maker committed on the same day, no email chain, no calendar tag. The deal isn't closed but the friction between now and close is minimal.
This is the cleanest version of the BDR handoff motion in the dataset. Document it as a named playbook: (1) identify decision-maker during discovery, (2) confirm current prospect has authority to commit, (3) book same-day or next-day handoff call while still on the live call, (4) physically confirm the time with the decision-maker before the first call ends. Zack did all four in under a minute.
Effective
McDonald's fries metaphor for lead speed-to-contact
Zack used Mike's line on this call: "Our VP of sales Mike tells us all the time — leads are like McDonald's French fries. In the first 15 minutes they're hot, crispy, fresh, delicious. After that they're soggy, cold, and gross." Fozia laughed, agreed, and described her clinic manager "resurrecting the dead" when leads go cold — which Zack then used as the setup for the automated follow-up story. This is the kind of vivid one-liner that sells the speed-to-lead point better than a statistic.
Add to Quick Ref as a named metaphor. Every rep should have it in their pocket for any lead-speed conversation.
Watch
Aria is the real decision-maker — Fozia is medical authority
Fozia is an owner and medical director, but she repeatedly deferred to Aria on operational decisions — follow-up, CRM, team workflow, and specifically asked Zack to run through the full pitch again with Aria later in the day. The deal will be decided on Aria's read, not Fozia's. This is a useful dynamic to recognize: when the clinical founder defers operationally to a manager, that manager is the actual buyer.
Going into the 4:30pm handoff call, the rep should anchor around: (1) automation as the solution to Aria's manual texting problem (her phone got flagged as spam by Apple from mass-texting 2,000-3,000 patients a day — real operational pain), (2) the multi-location dynamic Fozia mentioned late, (3) the 4,000-patient database as the immediate first-month opportunity. Aria will decide fast if those three hit.

The Ageless pitch writes itself: automate what Aria is doing manually, and route the 4,000-patient database through the tool in the first month.

The three things that should lead Aria's call: (1) the Apple spam flag as evidence that manual mass-texting has hit a literal ceiling, (2) the 4,000-patient database as the first-month unlimited-tier test case, (3) the multi-location dynamic that makes the tool a 2x asset rather than a 1x one.

Handoff brief for the 4:30pm call:
Aria is a consultant who was trained on the sales process from the beginning and built the current lead system. Don't sell her on the concept — she'll get it faster than most. Sell her on the specific automation story, the database blast math, and the skip-the-waitlist urgency for founders club. She's going to ask about GoHighLevel integration and multi-location setup. Have both answers ready.

What worked
Strong
Jennifer identified the decision committee in the first 30 seconds
Laura opened the call with "I've got Mimi and Russell here with me as well — my practice manager and operations manager." Jennifer clocked this immediately and adjusted her delivery to include all three, explicitly addressing Russell's operational questions and Mimi's implementation concerns throughout. This is the first full decision-committee call in the dataset and Jennifer handled it without fumbling the multi-stakeholder dynamic.
When a decision committee is present, identify each person's functional interest within the first minute and route specific parts of the pitch to each. Jennifer did this well with Russell (operations/integration) but could have been more deliberate about directing specific portions to Mimi (practice manager — patient flow, QR code placement, front desk workflow).
Strong
"How are you getting patients right now?" — asked cleanly, answered cleanly
Jennifer's opening discovery question was the right one for a 6-week-old practice. Laura answered honestly: mostly her own clientele and her team's existing clients, no paid ads yet, considering them. This framed the Ageless pitch perfectly — not as an upgrade to an existing acquisition stack but as the foundation of one. Jennifer pivoted smoothly: "That's essentially what Ageless is — we're a lead capture tool."
For brand-new practices with no established acquisition channel, Ageless becomes the acquisition channel. This is a different pitch from "add Ageless to what you're already doing" — it's "Ageless IS what you're doing." Worth flagging as a named use case alongside the pre-launch and new-location patterns.
Gaps
Gap
Podium's built-in AI communication — not fully addressed
Laura explicitly said "one of the reasons we chose podium was because of their robust AI communication component — I don't want to have two lead systems." This is a real concern and Jennifer's answer ("we bridge through Kerrigan or Zapier so leads filter into podium") was technically correct but didn't fully address the underlying worry: Laura doesn't want two AI systems competing for the same patient communication. The answer needs to differentiate Ageless's role (visualization + enrichment + scoring before the lead exists) from Podium's role (AI follow-up after the lead exists in their system).
The clean version: "Podium handles conversations with patients who are already in your system. Ageless creates those patients in the first place — visualization, scoring, enrichment, then hands them cleanly to Podium. The two systems don't compete because they operate at different stages of the funnel. You keep Podium as your patient communication layer. Ageless feeds it better-qualified leads with richer context than any form-fill can produce."
Well handled
"What happens if someone doesn't achieve the results in the pictures? Is there a disclaimer?"
Laura's exact version of the clinical liability objection — same category as Wenzel's but phrased more operationally. Jennifer handled it in two parts: (1) the AI simulation language in the flow, (2) three consent checkboxes patients sign before seeing any imaging. Laura followed up: "Is there like a disclaimer? Are they signing something saying they understand we're not legally obligated to get them with this exact look?" Jennifer confirmed yes, three checkboxes, built with the medical advisory board. This is the clean version of the answer.
The three-checkbox answer works for most clinical operators. For plastic surgeons (Wenzel), the reframe — "this actually reduces your liability because patients arrive with documented expectations" — is stronger. For non-surgical clinicians like Laura, the consent language alone is usually sufficient.
New objection — well handled
"Do you sell this to practices within 10 or 15 miles of us? Is there market exclusivity?"
Laura raised a question no other prospect in the dataset has: competitive market protection. Jennifer answered honestly — the product is white-labeled to the practice, so Ageless itself isn't the differentiator between two practices in the same market. She then pivoted to the first-to-market advantage: "One of the benefits of getting started sooner than later is it allows you to be first to market to your area." Laura accepted the answer.
Add this as a new objection in the Trust & Credibility section. The honest answer has two layers: (1) Ageless is white-labeled, so competitors using it won't have your brand, your link, your patient flow, or your consultation language; (2) there is a real first-mover advantage in a local market — the first practice to deploy Ageless creates the patient expectation that this is how aesthetic consultations work, and later competitors are chasing that experience rather than setting it.
Well handled
"Is there a commitment — six months, a year?"
Standard contract question, handled cleanly: month-to-month, no contracts, cancel inside the product. Laura was satisfied quickly.
Partial
"I don't want two lead systems — Podium handles this already"
Covered in Discovery gaps. The answer needs sharper differentiation between Ageless (pre-lead visualization + scoring) and Podium (post-lead communication). Jennifer's technical answer about Kerrigan/Zapier bridges was correct but missed the conceptual reframe that would have resolved Laura's underlying worry.
Bring this cleanly to the Wednesday close call. The reframe: "Podium and Ageless don't compete — they handle different stages. Podium runs your conversations with patients who already exist in your system. Ageless creates those patients from visitors who aren't in your system yet. Together they're a full funnel. Without Ageless you're missing the top of it."
Effective
Wednesday noon close call booked on the live call
Jennifer's closing move was textbook: rather than try to close three stakeholders on the first call, she confirmed interest ("sounds like we're pretty interested"), asked for a specific time block, and booked a 10-minute Wednesday noon call specifically for registration. The time boxing matters — Laura and Russell committed to 10 minutes, which is low-friction to agree to and usually expands into the full close once momentum is live.
The 10-minute close call is a strong pattern for decision-committee prospects. Frame it as: "Let me block 10 minutes to register you — if at that point you have more questions, we can go deeper. If not, we can get you set up in 5 minutes and I'll send the onboarding email the same day." Low-commitment ask, high-conversion outcome.
Watch
Follow-up email has to resolve the Podium/lead-system concern before Wednesday
Laura explicitly said she wants to "try it myself and let my injector look at it as well" before the Wednesday call. The recap email Jennifer committed to sending needs to (a) include the demo link (or a workaround), (b) preempt the Podium concern with the funnel-stage reframe, and (c) give Laura something to show the injector that's more compelling than a written description.
Build the recap email to carry specific weight for the Wednesday call: include the three-checkbox clinical liability answer with a screenshot, the Podium differentiator language, and the demo link. The injector needs to see something tangible between now and Wednesday or she'll push for another round of evaluation.

Mandy Smith — Vitality Health & Wellness
Apr 21, 2026  ·  38 min  ·  Lucy O’Brien + Mike Hourihan  ·  Small-town Georgia · Instagram-discovered · Mike-led rapport masterclass
✓ Closed — card on call, rapport-led close with zero real objection handling
▶ Gong

Rapport masterclass

Closing
Key moments
Profile
Teaching call
Why this call is in the War Room
This call closed with card on call despite Lucy dropping off the Zoom for nearly 5 minutes, despite zero real objection handling, and despite discovery being unusually light. The product pitch didn't close this deal —
cumulative rapport did
. Mike layered five distinct rapport moves throughout the call, each one small on its own, compounding into a trust relationship that converted a cold Instagram-discovered prospect into a same-call card within 38 minutes. This panel documents each move so they can be studied and reused.
The five rapport moves, in order
Move 1 — 1:02
The correction-to-connection pivot
Lucy introduced Mike as "Michael." Mike's first move: "You just called me Michael. Yeah... no, my sister's name is Mandy." He converted a micro-correction into a sister-connection in under five seconds. Mandy responded immediately with personal warmth ("it's a great name"). The rapport was established before the product pitch even started.
When anything minor goes sideways at the start of a call (name mispronunciation, late start, technical glitch), the move is to make the moment human, not to apologize and move on. Mike turned a correction into a connection. That tone then set the register for the entire call.
Move 2 — 1:22
Owning the prior bugging without groveling
Mike discovered the old office manager (who had taken multiple demos and gotten spammed with emails for months) was no longer with the practice. Most reps would apologize defensively or dodge the topic. Mike: "It looks like we bugged the crap out of you guys. Sorry for that." Direct acknowledgment, no grovel, a little self-aware humor. Mandy immediately pivoted to tell him the old email had been getting lots of stuff — she took the admission and expanded on it, which meant she trusted him enough to be honest back.
When the prospect could legitimately be annoyed at your company, name it directly and specifically. "We bugged the crap out of you" is the kind of honest admission that creates trust faster than "I apologize for any inconvenience." The mini-humor ("don't send us your new email" from Lucy) reinforced it.
Move 3 — 1:49
The noticed-detail compliment
Mandy's Zoom background had an AI-generated flower in her hair. Lucy complimented it first ("I love that little flower in your hair"). Mike extended it: "You look like you're very nice. Oh, outside of my house at the moment." He noticed the palm trees in her background and made it specific. Lucy was ready to move on; Mike stayed in the rapport one more beat. That one extra beat compounded.
When a rapport moment is working, don't be in a hurry to move on to business. The 30 seconds spent noticing a detail and commenting on it genuinely builds more trust than 30 minutes of product pitching. Mike read the room correctly — Mandy was enjoying the interaction, so he stayed in it longer.
Move 4 — 21:42
Using the technical failure as a rapport amplifier
Lucy's connection dropped at approximately 15:36. Mike was alone with Mandy for nearly 5 minutes while Lucy tried to reconnect. Rather than fill the silence with awkward small talk or apologize repeatedly for his colleague, Mike used the time to reinforce the sister connection when Lucy came back: "Lucy, I said my sister's name is Mandy. You're giving me flashbacks from having her hang up on me." Mandy laughed — "do we act alike?" — and the rapport hit was bigger because it had been reinforced twice across an interruption. The dropped call became a rapport amplifier, not a momentum killer.
Technical failures are opportunities, not setbacks, when the rapport is already warm. The move is to treat the interruption as normal life, not a crisis — and to use the moment to reinforce something established earlier. Mike's reference back to the sister callback demonstrated he was present and paying attention across the entire arc of the call.
Move 5 — 33:19
The spouse-humor close
When Mandy said "I'll buy my CFO aka my husband," Mike immediately: "And after that you can talk to my wife. So that she does stuff like that." Then layered on: "I'm not the CFO, I'm just the bank... and she's got an ATM card. So annoying." This landed so well that Lucy picked up the thread at 35:44 ("if your husband hates it, we'll just refund you the $499") and Mike kept it going ("now you're talking like my wife"). The spouse-humor made Mandy feel completely normal for deferring to her husband — which meant she could close the card on the call without feeling like the real decision was still to come. The deal closed because the spouse-approval gate felt like a shared joke rather than a real barrier.
When a prospect says they need to consult a spouse, match the energy. If it's genuine hesitation, take it seriously. If it's a normalized part of their decision process (as Mandy's tone clearly signaled), make it feel shared and light. Mike's self-deprecation — "I'm not the CFO, I'm just the bank" — made Mandy feel comfortable buying while the decision was technically still pending spouse review. That's how the card came in on a call that technically didn't have full decision authority.
The pattern
What cumulative rapport actually looks like
Each of these five moves on its own is minor. None of them is a trick or a technique. The teaching value is in the cumulative effect: five distinct rapport hits spread across a 38-minute call, each one tied to a specific moment in the conversation, each one building on the previous.

The result is a close that doesn't need objection handling — because the relationship has done the objection handling invisibly. Mandy didn't raise a beauty score concern. She didn't raise a clinical accuracy concern. She didn't ask for references. She didn't ask for a demo link. She asked about her husband, Mike made it human, and the card came in.

What this teaches:
Rapport isn't a warm-up phase to get through before the real pitch. It's the closing mechanism itself, sustained across the entire call. Product content fills the middle, but the trust that converts the prospect is established in minute 1 and compounded through minute 38.
Light
Discovery was minimal because it didn't need to be heavy
Lucy's core discovery: how are you getting patients today? Mandy: small-town Georgia, word of mouth, haven't needed paid acquisition. Mike's follow-up: "Have you ever kind of played that whole get pay some marketing agency, get a bunch of leads, see if you can call them game?" Mandy: "I haven't needed it really, honestly." Mike pivoted without pushing: "That's fair. Well, I think Ageless has a lot of uses and one of them is definitely in practice." This is the right move — when a prospect is self-sufficient on acquisition, don't try to sell them the acquisition use case. Sell them the in-practice and existing-patient use cases instead.
Read the prospect's acquisition posture early. Mandy didn't need lead generation; she needed cross-modality exposure and consultation enhancement. Mike pivoted to in-practice and waiting-room deployment without friction. For any word-of-mouth-dominant small-town practice, this is the right pitch direction.
Reframe
SEO surfaced — Mandy's marketing girl does it, she doesn't know details
Lucy noticed the practice was first in Google results for Ellicott City. Mandy credited her marketing person. This was a small moment but worth noting — Mandy is the medical side, not the marketing side. The buyer is someone who won't herself deploy Ageless; she'll hand it to her marketing person. The close needs to produce material Mandy can forward.
When the buyer isn't the operator, produce handoff material. Lucy committed to sending the call recording specifically so the marketing person could watch it. That's the right follow-up move for any buyer who won't personally implement the tool.
Effective
Card came in before full decision authority was resolved
At 33:19, Mandy says she needs her CFO/husband. Mike's spouse humor defuses the gate. At 35:44, Lucy closes with the refund fallback: "If your husband hates it, we'll just refund you the $499." Mandy accepts. Card comes in at 36:10. This is a sophisticated close — the $499 refund commitment converted a spouse-approval gate into a low-risk purchase. Mandy could say yes to her husband later ("I bought it, if you hate it we can cancel") rather than before the call ended.
The "if your [spouse/partner/boss] hates it, we'll refund" close is a powerful lever for prospects who have a stated approval dependency but are otherwise ready to commit. It only works when: (a) rapport is genuinely warm, (b) the purchase is low-risk (no contract, easy cancel), (c) the prospect signals soft rather than hard dependency. Lucy read all three correctly.
Effective
Recording commitment to forward to marketing agency
Mandy asked if she could forward the recording to her marketing person. Lucy committed to it immediately. This is a free amplification mechanism — the marketing person watches a call that's already gone well, sees the rapport, sees the product, sees the close happen, and becomes a second-order advocate for deployment. Zero extra rep time required.
Always offer the recording to any prospect who will forward it internally. Don't make them ask. For any call where the buyer isn't the operator, the recording handoff is the activation lever that converts a closed deal into an implemented deal.
0:27
AI flower on Mandy's Zoom:
Unintentional background detail. Lucy compliments it: "you should keep that." Light opening.
1:02
RAPPORT MOVE 1 — Sister connection:

1:22
RAPPORT MOVE 2 — Owning the bugging:

Mandy's call is the clearest rapport-led close in the dataset. The product demo was competent but not central to the close. Objections were nonexistent. Discovery was light. What closed the deal was five distinct rapport moves from Mike layered across 38 minutes, combined with Lucy's refund-safety-net close at the spouse-approval moment.

The teaching value of this call is rare. Most War Room calls document either a strong objection-handling moment (Becky, Mina) or a specific playbook execution (Fozia's BDR handoff, Dusan's event deadline). This one documents something harder to teach and harder to measure: what cumulative, sustained rapport actually produces. Worth studying specifically for reps who tend to lead with product pitch and treat rapport as a warm-up.

Strong
Shawn arrived pre-sold by Dr. Barbarino Instagram — warm inbound, correct handling
Shawn opened at 1:01 with the origin story: she met Dr. Barbarino at AmSpa last year, had a casual conversation before realizing Barbarino was a keynote speaker, and has been following her Instagram since. When Barbarino started posting about Ageless, Shawn clocked it as a signal to explore. This is the cleanest documented example of the advisory board doing its job — a prospect arriving pre-warmed because an advisory board member they respect is publicly associated with the product. Lucy recognized the advantage and didn't run heavy discovery; Mike reinforced the Barbarino connection naturally ("she does a lot of fun Instagram stuff"). The call moved to demo inside 7 minutes because it didn't need anything else.
When a prospect names an advisory board member as the reason they're on the call, compress discovery and go straight to product. The credibility work is already done. The only discovery question that matters is what they're currently doing and what's the deployment anchor. Everything else is over-selling a pre-sold prospect.
Strong
Mike surfaced Podium's Jan 2026 EMR launch live during the call — competitive intel delivered as news, not attack
At 5:29, Mike asked Shawn what CRM she used. When she said Podium, Mike said: "they've made some interesting changes lately, huh? For new customers, Podium just launched their own EMR. They're not allowing people to use anything else but that." Shawn was surprised — she hadn't heard this from Podium yet. Mike delivered the intel factually, didn't bash Podium, and let Shawn absorb the implication herself (if Podium forces EMR migration, her Aesthetic Record setup is at risk). She said explicitly: "If that happens, I might move to something else." This is how competitive intel lands without tripping the ethical-rep alarm — factual, recent, offered as a data point rather than a pitch.
The Podium January 2026 AI OS for Aesthetics launch is now documented in the Battle Cards tab with the detail that their EMR is becoming a required component. Any rep on a Podium-using prospect call should mention this factually early. The goal is not to attack Podium — it's to surface the operational risk so the prospect can weight it themselves. Shawn's "I might move to something else" response is what this move produces when done well.
Strong
Mike caught the Saturday pilates event and turned it into the deployment deadline
Toward the close, Shawn mentioned she's vendoring at a pilates event on Saturday — she already brings her facial skin analyzer and Styku body scanner. Mike immediately connected the dots: Ageless QR codes on her swag bag cards would let attendees take their experience home via email rather than leaving with just an in-practice scan. Shawn lit up: she was already tired of "schlepping stuff around." Mike turned Saturday into the deadline that justifies immediate signup: "If Lucy, would you have time this afternoon just to... because I want to know, I mean, I know it's kind of crunch time, would I have that QR code for Saturday for my event? Yes — we can get you live by then."
For any prospect with a concrete upcoming event (open house, conference, pilates event, party, launch), surface it early in discovery and anchor close urgency around it. Saturday turned into a soft deadline that produced a 1pm same-day close call. The pattern: find the event, make deployment by the event the specific commitment, and compress the sales cycle around it.
Gap
Growth99 integration question not fully answered — Shawn surfaced her frustration without a clean resolution
Late in the call at 49:27, Shawn went on a frustration tangent about Growth99: "I'm so mad at Growth99 right now. I'm like I don't have time to micromanage you people." She had just discovered her own website had a self-assessment form in the upper-right corner she didn't even know was there — nobody was using it because it was badly placed. Mike validated her frustration and pointed out the placement problem as evidence that Growth99 isn't managing her tool well. The integration question ("would they integrate Ageless into my website?") got answered: yes, Growth99 is a partner and sends leads to Ageless. But the specific implementation mechanic wasn't walked through live.
For Growth99-hosted website prospects, have a specific embed walkthrough ready — Growth99 is a partner but practices still need operational clarity on which URL goes where and whether Growth99's team handles the embed or the practice does. This came up with Jess (removed from War Room but the gap is real) and now Shawn. Worth tightening the answer before the Friday close call.
Handled well
"I feel like I'm losing control of the patient experience"
Shawn's concern at 45:11 is the MD/NP control-of-consult version of the under-promise objection. She said: "When I have them in my chair, I have a captive audience. If they do this, I just feel like I'm losing control of the patient experience." Mike walked her through the reframe — the goal isn't to replace the consultation, it's to fill the consultation with a patient who already has context and interest rather than cold. The alternative to Ageless for a prospect who doesn't engage is them leaving the website silently and never becoming a patient at all. Mike's line: "The alternative to that is them probably going to your website, and then leaving and you never even knew it." Shawn accepted this and moved to pricing.
The control-of-consult objection is the operator-led variant of the under-promise concern. The reframe is not "you'll still have control" — it's "the alternative to Ageless is invisibility." Frame the current state as the problem (prospects leaving silently) rather than defending Ageless's role in the consultation. This lands with operators who take pride in their in-chair consultation experience — which is most of the NP and solo-operator prospects.
Effective
Saturday event created the same-day close urgency naturally

This is the cleanest same-day close structure in the dataset — the prospect's own event became the reason to move now, and the rep team recognized it and anchored the close accordingly.
Same-day close calls are the right structure when the prospect has surfaced a concrete upcoming event. The pattern: identify the event, confirm the deployment window fits, offer a specific time later the same day (not "tomorrow," not "next week"), and put it on their calendar live. Mike putting the 1pm call on Shawn's calendar during the demo closed the loop before the conversation ended.

For prospects who cite a specific advisory board member as their trigger, make the founders WhatsApp community connection to that member explicit in the close. "Dr. Barbarino is active in the WhatsApp group — when you join you'll be in the same community as her" is significantly stronger than a generic "access to the advisory board" pitch.

Strong
Yaman has VISIA in-practice — Mike positioned Ageless as complementary, not competitive

This is the exact framing the updated VISIA battle card uses — VISIA for skin-focused in-practice consults, Ageless for full-menu acquisition before they ever book. Yaman accepted the complementary framing and moved on.
VISIA is a common in-practice tool — always position Ageless as the upstream acquisition layer, not the replacement. The VISIA investment is real and practices don't want to hear it replaced. "VISIA closes, Ageless fills" is the right language. Updated battle card uses this exact framing.
Strong
Remy correctly adapted the pitch when Yaman named his limited-volume target
When Yaman said he wanted "maybe five patients per month but higher quality" — not volume — Remy pivoted cleanly. This matches Fozia's "click happy people" framing exactly. The pitch shifted from "drive more traffic" to "convert the traffic you already have with better scoring." For practices that have already tried volume and been burned, this is the right positioning.
Low-volume high-quality-seeking prospects (fired-an-agency, anti-Groupon, values-forward) need the "fewer better leads" pitch, not the "flood of leads" pitch. Match the volume expectation to their pain tolerance. This is a named pattern now across Fozia, Yaman, and Jennifer Milan.
Beauty score — values pushback
"The beauty level is very subjective and offensive to some people"
At 21:12, Yaman raised the beauty score as a hard concern: "the beauty score, I mean, I think that's very subjective and offensive... if they think of their self at a certain level and you come below it, I think you're offending them right there and then." This is a clean pattern match with Becky (TOPS) and Mina (Lamb Medical) — both values-forward operators, both rejected the score. Mike confirmed the toggle-off setting exists. Remy mentioned the viral-moment counter-argument (some practices love it). Yaman accepted the toggle as the answer.
The beauty-score-toggleable coaching pattern continues to hold. For values-forward operators (read website language: "empowerment," "celebrate yourself," "your natural beauty"), pre-empt the objection in discovery by naming the toggle early: "the beauty score is optional — some practices love the viral moment, some turn it off. Both work." Yaman is the 3rd case now for this pattern (Becky, Mina, Yaman).
Financing integration gap
"Do you connect to Cherry or CareCredit for pre-qualification?"
At 38:40, Yaman asked about financing integrations. Mike's answer was honest: Ageless integrates with Affirm via RepeatMD only — no Cherry, no CareCredit, no Klarna. For RepeatMD customers, the as-low-as pricing links out to Affirm. For non-RMD customers, financing isn't integrated into Ageless. Yaman noted this as a limitation. He has Cherry on his website already and wanted pre-qual flow built into the Ageless consult experience.
Cherry and CareCredit are the two most common financing platforms for medspas (Cherry approval rates are high, CareCredit has brand recognition). Ageless doesn't integrate with them today. This is worth flagging to product — for non-RMD Ageless customers, a "financing options" link that routes to their preferred platform (editable in admin) would close this gap. For now, sales reps should acknowledge the gap honestly and offer the workaround: link to their existing financing page in follow-up messaging.
Deferred to team
Yaman wants injector + esthetician on the next call — team buy-in before commit

This is the team-buy-in deferral pattern — the owner has decided but wants his practitioners to experience the demo and not feel the tool was forced on them. It's not a brush-off; it's a legitimate management practice. Remy offered to send the call recording so the team could pre-watch at 2x speed, which is the right move for accelerating the next call.
For team-buy-in deferrals, offer the Gong recording at 2x speed pre-read before the next meeting. This compresses the next demo — the injector/esthetician have already seen the product walkthrough and can come in with focused questions. Booking the follow-up explicitly on Yaman's calendar (not "I'll send you a link") is worth doing before ending the call. Remy committed to emailing Yaman and connecting through his assistant — worth tightening to a specific time slot if possible.
Missed

This is the right answer — founders club pricing is the concession, not discounting. But the follow-up move — offering sign-up-and-pause for Yaman's team review — wasn't attempted. He could have locked founders pricing today with activation paused until after the team buy-in call.
For team-buy-in deferrals, offer sign-up-and-pause as the way to preserve founders pricing during the review period. "Lock $499 founders today, activation starts after your team call next week when you're ready to deploy" removes the pricing-risk pressure without pushing the team review. Yaman was warm enough that this likely would have landed. Worth coaching as the standard deferred-deal move.

Strong

Sign-up-and-pause continues to convert. The pattern is now demonstrated across three close types: Carrie (website redesign 4-6 week delay), Jen Roy (Saturday event expedite with JT), and Karthik (1-week wife ramp-up). For any deployment-delay reason (days, weeks, or months), the lever applies. Worth promoting to standard closing sequence: after pricing, ask "when would you want to go live?" — if the answer is anything other than "today/tomorrow," offer paused activation while locking pricing.
Well handled
"There were many bad reviews about RepeatMD"
Karthik opened with this explicitly at 1:56. Alex's response pattern (acknowledge specifically → own the growing-pain context → pivot to what's different about Ageless) landed cleanly. Karthik moved to product questions. Worth noting this was the second time today Alex converted this specific objection — the pattern is reliable.
This is becoming a standard early-call objection from RMD-aware prospects. Have the pattern ready: "yeah, we had contract issues — 24 and 36 month contracts were too long, we learned, and Ageless has no contract at all." Short, owned, pivoting to the structural fix. Don't defend or deflect.
Well handled
"Who owns the data? What happens if we cancel?"
At 50:25, Karthik asked: "Who owns the data? Like all these pictures and contacts?" Alex confirmed: "All the patient data belongs to you guys. We don't share data with anybody. No selling data to anybody." Follow-up: "If we cancel after first month, do we still have access to the information?" Alex: "Yes, 30-day window to download to CSV." Clean, direct, factual answers.
Data ownership is a predictable MD/NP-led prospect concern. Practices with any clinical component tend to hold data rights tightly. The clean answer: "Your data belongs to you, we don't sell or share, 30-day export window if you cancel." Worth having this memorized verbatim for every prospect. Karthik's satisfaction was immediate.
Well handled
"What's the cancellation window? 30 days before? Any opt-out period?"
At 33:48, Karthik asked specifically about cancellation terms. Alex's answer: "It's anytime. There's no opt-out period. You can cancel at any time." Karthik: "I'm telling you we did." Small laugh — he was implicitly testing whether the "no contract" claim would hold up when pressed. It did. Clean.
RMD-aware prospects will test the no-contract claim by asking about cancellation windows. Answer directly: "anytime, no opt-out period, no window." Any qualifier weakens the answer. If a prospect is skeptical of the no-contract claim, the shortest possible answer is the most trustworthy.
Effective
Sign-up-and-pause activated for 1-week delay — pricing locked, activation waits for wife
Karthik's wife needed 1 week for ramp-up. Alex locked founders pricing today, paused activation 1 week. This is a clean, short-timeline version of the sign-up-and-pause close. Compare to Carrie (weeks delay) and Jen Roy (days earlier via JT expedite) — the same lever works across the full range of timing variations. For Karthik specifically, this removed the "we're not ready yet" hesitation without losing founders pricing.
Sign-up-and-pause at 1-week granularity is now documented. The lever works for deployment delays from a few days (expedite the other direction via CTO relationship) to multiple months (pause activation until target date). Make this the default response to any timing hesitation in close.
Effective
Alex offered to record and send the call for the wife — clean family-team handoff
At 44:35, Karthik asked: "Is there a way after this thing is done, you can send a recording to my wife and she can see the whole video?" Alex: "Yes, absolutely." This is the Gong-recording-as-internal-pre-read move that was documented on Yaman's call. For family-team practices where the decision-maker isn't the operator, sending the Gong recording is the cleanest asynchronous handoff. The wife can watch at 2x and come in with specific questions rather than requiring Alex to re-demo the product.
Offer Gong recording proactively for any spouse/partner/team handoff situation. "I can send you the recording so [wife/team] can watch at 2x speed" is a high-value offer that saves everyone's time. Worth making standard for family-team and multi-stakeholder close situations.

Tactical fit
Targeted Botox/contouring ads with high conversion already running

For practices already converting on paid, don't re-sell the funnel. Sell the quality of the lead and the cross-sell opportunity.
Objections
Values-forward
AI over-corrected the under-eyes even though under-eyes weren't in the plan

Documented "validate then pivot" in the wild. When the prospect catches a real AI limitation, say "I see the point" — don't argue the AI's accuracy. The product is good enough to survive acknowledgment.
Privacy
"Do you have to get permission to get all their financial information?"
At 20:52 Tina asked about consent for the income/net-worth enrichment. Lucy's answer: "This is all based on publicly available information... we're not pulling tax returns, it's based on LinkedIn, profession, Instagram."
Sharp answer worth memorizing. The distinction is public-domain scraping (LinkedIn, social) vs. private records (tax, medical). Say both parts.
Trial ask
