Standard hold-and-pivot. The unlimited first month IS the trial. Naming it as such ("this is effectively your trial month with skin in the game") is worth adding to the script.
Close
Pre-empt
Beauty score toggle pre-empted BEFORE Tina raised it

Pre-empt is more powerful than react. When you read Track C in discovery, mention the toggle DURING the demo flow, not after they push back.

Archetype

She runs GHL in-house, manages Boulevard for existing patients, handles all marketing personally. This is a new archetype — the sophisticated operator who knows systems as well as services.

Complementary

This is the third documented complementary-positioning execution with an in-room analyzer (after VISIA with Dusan/Yaman/Jen Platinum). Language that works: "That's your consultation-room tool. Ageless is the acquisition-room tool." The prospect often solves this for you if you let them.
Strategic frame

Discovery
Objections
Closing
Key moments
Profile
Discovery highlights
Active frustration
Bren opened with a detailed RMD failure story — including account manager going silent on her wedding
From 2:38 Bren described months of failure: Shelly set up incorrectly, David corrected later, account manager Tony went on wedding leave without handoff, Bren rolling out "falling for beauty" campaign from Europe found it broken, blamed for misunderstanding. "I was like, I'm going to fucking lose my mind." Remy opened with empathy, not defensiveness: "a lot of people never, you know, they just go. So they don't say anything."
When an existing customer is actively angry, the discovery IS them telling you what went wrong. Don't interrupt. Don't defend. Listen, validate, let the pain fully surface before pivoting.
Peer philosophy
Mike's restaurant parallel — Danny Meyer / intentional tray drops
At 5:59 Mike pivoted into a story about Danny Meyer (Shake Shack, Gramercy Tavern) and how some restaurants intentionally drop trays in front of customers to rehearse service recovery. It elevated the conversation from sales call to philosophical exchange between business operators who both value service.
Peer-level storytelling at the start of a recovery call is the trust builder. Don't apologize and pivot — apologize AND demonstrate you understand service philosophy at their level.
Full acknowledgment
Mike said what she needed to hear: "We should treat you better then"
At 7:38 Mike dropped the recovery line: "Well, you know, we should treat you better then. I mean, like, look, you're doing some really good things." Direct acknowledgment of service failure, paired with validation of her business. No deflection, no "let me connect you with someone," no defensive clarification.
The phrase "we should treat you better" is a recovery unlock. It's an ownership statement. Remember: you are not responsible for the original failure, but you represent the company now. Owning it is the price of rebuilding.
Objections
Trust
Why should I give you money for a second product after the first one failed?
This was the implicit objection throughout. Addressed by (1) not pressing the sale early — spent 15+ minutes on rapport and acknowledgment, (2) Mike offering to personally oversee the Ageless setup ("I'm happy to take a look"), (3) tying the Ageless close to extended RMD benefits rather than a new transaction.
Trust objection cannot be handled with features. It's handled with time, ownership, and proof of personal accountability. Don't rush the close on a recovery call — rushing signals the same transactional behavior that created the problem.
Incrementality (Mike raised it himself)
At 50:54 Mike raised the uncomfortable truth: "is this incremental or not?"
"Like if you're finding that it looks like 14K that's gone through repeatmd is all just people that would have paid you anyway and not incremental, you need to make that decision because 700 or 500 whatever it is a month is probably not worth it for just convenience." Mike voiced the hardest possible question against his own sale. Bren's response: full trust, bought Ageless, kept RMD.
Uncomfortable truth as a closing lever. Documented pattern now. When you voice the hardest critique of your own product out loud, you earn the right to recommend the right product. Use when the prospect already suspects it.
Close
Choice architecture
Mike structured the close as "try Ageless, keep RMD on complimentary terms, decide in July"
At 37:15 Mike framed it: "If you like ageless, let's try it. Take the free months of repeatmd and give it another shot. But I mean, I don't know, I think after that period's over, you should take a hard look at like is this incremental or not." The close wasn't "buy both." It was "try both, revisit when you have data." That's the recovery structure.
Recovery closes use choice architecture, not commitment pressure. Give the prospect a decision point in the future with no penalty for either path. That's the trust rebuild in structural form.
Damage credit
Remy offered an extra free month on RMD, Mike upped it to three
At 1:01:10 Remy: "I'm going to add an extra month credit for you on repeat MD end." Mike: "If it was one month, I was definitely gonna make it three." The credit was a material damage acknowledgment — not discount negotiation.
Damage credits in recovery calls are not discounts — they're ownership signals. Offer them proactively, not in response to a pushback. Timing matters: AFTER the close on the new product, not before.
Card on call
Ageless card captured at 59:18 — full close despite the long recovery arc
Bren provided her card details live. Live by Saturday. The RMD credit conversation happened AFTER the Ageless close, not as a negotiation lever before it.
In recovery calls, the sequence is: empathize → acknowledge → philosophically connect → offer the uncomfortable truth → close the new product → THEN offer damage credit on the old product. Reverse this order and it reads as bribery.

This call worked because of three structural moves: (1) Remy and Mike spent 12+ minutes on rapport and acknowledgment before any pitch — the discovery was Bren venting, (2) Mike delivered the philosophical parallel (Danny Meyer / intentional tray drops) that reframed the call as peer-to-peer service dialogue rather than sales call, (3) Mike voiced the hardest possible objection to his own sale at 50:54 — asking whether the $500/mo RMD had been incremental at all — which earned trust and produced the close. The close structure itself matters: Ageless signed first, RMD credit offered AFTER the close (not as lever). This is now the documented RMD recovery motion: empathize → acknowledge → philosophically connect → uncomfortable truth → close new product → damage credit on old. Card-on-call captured at 59:18.

Archetype
Emily self-identifies: "I work in the industry too — I'm a sales rep in aesthetics"
At 1:22 Emily volunteered: "I'm a sales rep in aesthetics as well. And I know of one of my accounts that she just started using it." This is the industry-insider archetype — the prospect has sold into med spas before and knows the vocabulary, the objections, the competitive landscape.
When you hear "I work in the industry" or "I'm a rep for [device manufacturer]" — shift to peer-level. Don't explain what AI is or what lead gen does. Ask how they'd position it to their own customers.
Peer move
Mike: "You sell stuff already. So, you know."
At 7:20 Mike hit the exact peer reframe: "You sell stuff already. So, you know." Emily: "I think I know too much and I've done this for a long time and I've seen a lot of businesses be successful and I've seen a lot of businesses fail." Mike validated without selling: "No shade on that. I understand the industry."
Two-sentence peer unlock. For industry-insider prospects, the move is explicit acknowledgment that they know the game. Don't try to teach them — respect them.
Google-first advice
Mike gave genuinely useful advice unprompted: strong Google listing + reviews beats paid ads
At 9:40 Mike: "For your money, the best advertisement is having a good strong Google business listing... there's a lot of pressure on like meta ads but I mean for my money it's free to be on Google." Advice given freely, with no Ageless tie-in. Emily responded "that's kind of where we're at."
With industry insiders, give advice that helps them independent of your product. It establishes credibility and earns permission for the pitch. Bad advice or self-serving advice fails instantly with this archetype.
Objections
Under-promise
Emily: "I don't want to set up false expectations especially for any facial procedures"
At 27:59 Emily raised the concern directly: "I never want to set up false expectations. I wouldn't want anybody to feel underwhelmed." Mike handled with the advisory board framing. Stefanie then surfaced conservative AI mode at 30:35: "if you wanted to keep the results more conservative, you can turn on the ageless AI conservative mode too."
Conservative AI mode is now a documented feature-level objection handler. Put it in the objections tab. For values-forward / under-promise prospects, this is the specific lever.
Sophistication test
Emily: "What is in the future for the company? Where do you guys see it going?"
At 46:47 Emily asked the sophisticated-operator tell: where is the company going? Mike's answer was honest and grounded: "I don't even know if I could predict what's going to happen in 6 months or a year... we'd be foolish to sort of plan what's next." He named one direction (directory / consolidation) and one speculative (virtual advisor) but refused to oversell.
The "what's next" question from industry insiders is a test. Overselling = lost credibility. Honest uncertainty = trust. If you can name 1-2 directions without committing, that's the answer. "I don't know exactly" is better than "let me tell you our 3-year roadmap."

So we just have to discuss timing too." This is a textbook sign-up-and-pause trigger — capital constraint + timing uncertainty. Mike extended the deadline to Monday but did not offer to lock pricing with a paused activation.
The capital-reshuffling signal ("we have some funds coming free") is a SUAP trigger. Script: "lock founders pricing today, activation whenever your social media budget frees up." Worth adding to the trigger list explicitly.

For practices with strong consultation-room tools, name BOTH acquisition and in-practice engagement. Don't pick one.
Extension
Extended the AmSpa promo deadline to Monday for partner review
"Why don't we just give you till Monday" at 52:44. Mike granted extension without tying it to a commitment. Standard practice but fragile — Monday is a long way away without a lock.
Deadline extensions are not closes. Without a SUAP or a specific next-step action, "Monday" is optional. Pair every extension with either a SUAP offer or a specific prep action (share the recording, send internal memo, etc.).

Key moments
Profile
Why this call is different from every other in the dataset
Unique
This wasn't an inbound Ageless demo — it was a support rescue that became one
Rachelle and Anthony booked this call trying to reach their RepeatMD CSM. Their account manager Shelly had left in February, the handoff was botched, and they had been circling Elizabeth for weeks without getting to anyone useful. Dr. Martin scheduled the Ageless demo email as a last resort to reach someone at the company — not because she was shopping for a new tool.

Mike identified the CS gap within the first two minutes, acknowledged it directly, committed to fixing it, and then pivoted into an Ageless demo without losing the room. Dr. Martin joined mid-call, saw the demo, and the call ended with a closed deal and a retention offer that kept them on RepeatMD. This is arguably the most complex call in the dataset — simultaneously a CS rescue, an existing customer upsell, and a new product demo.
Context
The CS frustration was real and needed to be addressed first — Mike did this correctly
Rachelle and Anthony had been bounced between contacts for weeks. They were paying for RepeatMD and not seeing ROI, couldn't get their rep on the phone, and had built up genuine frustration. Mike acknowledged it immediately, looked up the account in real time, identified the specific failure (Shelly leaving in February, handoff to Nicole not communicated), apologized without defensiveness, and committed to fixing it before the call ended.

This is the right sequence. Trying to pivot to a demo before the CS issue was acknowledged would have lost the room. Dr. Martin's comment — "like hitting the lottery, my God, you're going to help us with this" — was a direct result of Mike solving their immediate problem before selling them anything new.
Note
Recording paused mid-call — custom retention offer not in the transcript
At 36:37, Mike paused the Gong recording to deliver a custom offer: 3 months free RepeatMD in exchange for staying on the platform and adding Ageless. This is intentionally not in the public tool. The offer was the right move — the practice was at churn risk and the combined deal gave them a reason to lean in on both products rather than question whether they were getting value from either.
Discovery — scored low structurally, not because of rep execution
Structural
Discovery score of 4/10 reflects the call context, not Stefanie's ability
Standard discovery questions — what are you doing for acquisition, where's the disconnect, how do you follow up — were largely not asked because the first 12 minutes of the call were consumed by the CS situation. By the time Stefanie took over the demo, Dr. Martin had just joined and the priority was momentum, not excavation. The discovery score reflects the structural constraint of this call type, not a rep failure.
Strong
Mike got the key acquisition facts quickly inside a messy open
Despite the CS chaos, Mike still asked "How do you guys get new leads now — do you do any meta ads?" at 4:37. Anthony confirmed meta and Instagram, described the landing page flow, and Rachelle explained that she handles all inbound scheduling. Mike used this to set context for how Ageless fits in: "That just gives me a great idea of kind of where this fits in." Two questions, enough context to hand to Stefanie cleanly.
Efficient under pressure. When a call opens in chaos, get the minimum viable discovery to contextualize the demo, then hand off. Mike did exactly this.
Strong
Mike surfaced the RepeatMD underutilization problem — and turned it into a setup
Dr. Martin was candid: "I'm spending a lot every month and it seems like we only really use it for the financing." This is a churn signal. Mike didn't minimize it — he validated it, gave a concrete playbook for how to use RepeatMD properly (staff training, QR codes, obsessive check-in language, gift card promotions), and then connected Ageless as the acquisition layer that feeds the retention system they already have. The existing RepeatMD investment became a reason to add Ageless rather than a reason to doubt the category.
This is the correct framing for upselling Ageless to existing RepeatMD customers. "You already have the retention engine. Ageless is what fills it with the right patients in the first place."
Gaps
Missed
No discovery on practice size, revenue, or patient volume
The call never surfaced how many locations RegenRx has, what their monthly patient volume is, what their highest-revenue services are, or what they charge for the non-surgical facelift Anthony mentioned. This information would have helped frame the ROI math at pricing time and sharpen the demo toward their most relevant services.
Even in a rescue call, get at minimum: how many locations, how many providers, and what's your highest-ticket service. Three questions, 90 seconds, dramatically sharpens the pitch.
Missed
Wrong website pulled up in prep — acupuncture site instead of RegenRx
Stefanie and Mike had pulled up a Liu acupuncture website that was incorrectly associated with Dr. Martin's account. The confusion surfaced mid-demo and cost a few minutes of awkward backtracking. This is a pre-call prep issue — the account in the CRM had incorrect website data that nobody caught before the call.
Standard pre-call checklist should include: verify practice name, website, and service menu from the actual website, not just the CRM record. Takes 60 seconds and prevents this.
Closing mechanics
Effective
Product demo landed cleanly despite chaotic open
Despite the first 12 minutes being consumed by CS issues, Stefanie ran a clean demo sequence once she took over. Dr. Martin's reactions were enthusiastic throughout — "Oh my God, I love this," "Give me everything," "I love him" (re: Rajani). By the time pricing came up, the room was warm. The demo did its job.
Worth noting: Dr. Martin revealed she had the idea for this exact product 10-12 years ago. That's an unusually strong buy-in signal — she wasn't being sold on a concept, she was watching her own idea come to life. Stefanie could have leaned into that harder as a closing moment.
Effective
Recording pause + custom retention offer converted a churn risk into a two-product close
Pausing the recording to deliver a bespoke offer — 3 months free RepeatMD plus Ageless at $499 — was the right call for this situation. The practice was paying for RepeatMD without seeing ROI and was at real churn risk. The combined offer gave them a reason to recommit to the platform and add the new product simultaneously. Rachelle's email an hour later confirmed they were moving forward.
This is the correct playbook for existing RepeatMD customers who are underutilizing: acknowledge the gap, fix the CS issue, offer a retention bridge, and introduce Ageless as the thing that makes the existing investment worthwhile. The sequence matters — don't offer the retention deal before fixing the CS issue or it reads as a bribe.
Effective
Mike leveraged personal connections to add value beyond the demo
Mike committed to three concrete follow-through items during the call: (1) connect them with their CSM, (2) have his wife's contact at PatientNow reach out to help with their EMR setup, (3) text his wife upstairs in real time to initiate the PatientNow connection. All three happened on the call, not as vague promises. Dr. Martin's "hitting the lottery" reaction was as much about the problem-solving as the product demo.
Concrete deliverables made on the call — not promised for later — are a powerful trust accelerant. This is replicable: when you can solve an adjacent problem for a prospect during the call, do it visibly and immediately.
Watch
Dr. Martin left before the close — Rachelle and Anthony carried it
Dr. Martin had to leave at 33 minutes for a doctor's appointment. The final pricing discussion, the offer details, and the closing mechanics all happened with Rachelle and Anthony — not the medical director. The close ultimately came through Rachelle's email an hour later after she briefed Dr. Martin. The deal closed, but it required the office manager to successfully relay a custom offer to the decision-maker without the rep in the room.
When the decision-maker leaves mid-call, immediately shift to "here's what you need to tell her" mode. Give Rachelle a clean one-sentence summary of the offer before she hangs up: "When you talk to Dr. Martin, the offer is $499 for Ageless with 3 months free on RepeatMD — tell her that's what we put together for her specifically." Make it easy to relay accurately.

Gaps
Gap
~15 minutes on peptides, TRT, and GLP-1s — rapport-building that ran long
Matt is a self-described science geek who had just started tessamorelin. Mike matched his energy and the conversation went deep — peptide mechanisms, oral TRT vs. injections, Mounjaro vs. Ozempic, Mike's own diabetes management. The conversation was genuine and Matt clearly enjoyed it. But it consumed roughly a third of the call and the demo was compressed as a result. When a prospect signals deep domain expertise, match their energy for 2-3 minutes to establish the connection, then redirect: "I could talk about this all day — let me show you something that's actually going to make you even more interested in peptides from a practice growth angle." Gap

Well handled

This will come up on every existing RMD customer call. Have the one-line answer ready: "It's a separate product and a separate bill — $499/month, no contract, cancels in the product. It integrates with your RepeatMD dashboard so you see everything in one place."
Well handled

This objection has come up on multiple calls. The cleanest version of the answer: "It'll be a separate marketing number through Twilio — same setup as RepeatMD. Most practices use it for automated texts and make personal calls from their own phone. You can also just toggle off the auto-texting and handle follow-up yourself if you prefer."
Well handled

This is a good standard practice for Friday closes where onboarding is Monday. Proactively clarifying when billing starts eliminates a common stall: "I don't want to pay for a week I can't use."

Why this call is in the dataset despite the low discovery score
Unique

Context
Jamey is a low-engagement prospect — one of the more taciturn in the dataset
Jamey's answers across the call are short, non-committal, and often monosyllabic. "Yeah." "Okay." "I don't know." "Yeah, we do some hormones and peptides, okay?" He's not disengaged — he signed up and is paying — he's just a low-verbal prospect. This is worth naming because it changes how the call should be evaluated. A "good discovery call" with a talkative prospect can look like a failure with Jamey; conversely, a rep who over-interprets Jamey's brevity as disinterest will bail too early on a warm buyer.

Dominique handled this correctly — she didn't mistake his short answers for resistance, she kept the demo moving, and she read the close signal ("Yeah, let's try that") at minute 17 without needing more verbal affirmation. That's a specific skill.
What worked
Strong
Dominique asked about services and lead gen in the first 90 seconds
"Are you currently doing anything right now to get new leads in the door?" and "What services are your top sellers right now?" — both asked inside the first two minutes before the demo started. Jamey's answers were thin ("just the normal things, but I need something with befores and afters") but Dominique extracted enough to customize the demo: botox, fillers, CO2, microderm, RF, weight loss. Enough to run a relevant demo.
For low-verbal prospects, the two-question opener Dominique used is about the right depth — pushing for more detail likely wouldn't have gotten more detail. The right move with Jamey is to use his list of services as the structure for the demo and trust that his interest is being signaled non-verbally through demo engagement, not verbal elaboration.
Gaps
Gap
No discovery on practice structure, volume, CRM, or acquisition channels
Beyond services, almost nothing else surfaced. Who else works at the practice. How many patients a month. What he pays for marketing today. What's in his RMD admin already. Whether anyone else is involved in the decision. All absent. With a low-verbal prospect this can feel unavoidable — but it means the account is going to hit onboarding with Dominique having almost no context on who she's supporting.
With a taciturn prospect, the discovery trick is to ask yes/no or multiple-choice questions instead of open-ended ones. "Are you running paid ads or mostly word of mouth?" gets more signal than "tell me about your marketing." "Is it just you or do you have a front desk handling follow-up?" gets more than "who's on your team?" Jamey would have given clear short answers to the right question formats.
Gap
RMD context never leveraged — existing customer, never discussed
Jamey is an existing RepeatMD customer — the opening line confirmed it — but the entire call treated him as a cold Ageless prospect. No question about what he's currently using RMD for, whether he's activated, whether there are RMD assets (members, rewards, database) that Ageless should connect to. At 18:05 Jamey himself asked about marketing materials for the office and patient outreach — a perfect window to connect Ageless to his existing RMD member base, which Dominique handled as a general answer rather than using to pull in RMD specifics.
This is the same gap pattern flagged on Matt, Stephanie, and Joyce — existing RMD customers are a distinct call type that needs its own discovery opener. One question: "How are you currently using RepeatMD and what's working well for you?" Takes 60 seconds, surfaces activation state, opens the cross-product positioning. This should be a standard early question whenever an RMD flag appears on the account.
The payment failure sequence — 14 minutes of friction
Breakdown
Four separate payment attempts failed in sequence on a live Zoom
The sequence: (1) new debit card entered, "doesn't look like it went through on our end"; (2) same card re-entered, declined by bank block; (3) switched to ACH bank account via texted link; (4) Stripe flow hit Family Security Credit Union integration issue — "account number could not be accessed at this time"; (5) eventually hit Jamey's daily transfer limit. Roughly 14 of the 36 minutes were spent on this. The tape is genuinely painful to listen to.

Dominique stayed calm throughout, which is the only reason the deal survived. But multiple things compounded the failure: the debit card was new (Jamey says this early), her "Family Security Credit Union" is a small regional bank that often has weaker Stripe integration, and Jamey's daily ATM/debit limit was already partially consumed by paying bills that morning.
Strong
Dominique's composure saved the deal — didn't escalate the friction
At every failure point, Dominique's response was "let me try that again" or "let's try a different way" — she never expressed frustration, never made the prospect feel the failure was their fault, never gave up. At the 34-minute mark when nothing was working, she said "let me call my boss and see what we can do" and committed to texting him an extended link tomorrow. That move converted a failed close into a warm handoff rather than a lost deal.
This is worth studying. When a payment flow breaks, the rep's posture determines whether the deal survives. Dominique's specific moves: (1) didn't blame the prospect, (2) offered alternatives without panicking, (3) kept conversation going during waits, (4) committed to a concrete next step with a time, (5) gave her personal cell so Jamey had direct access. All five matter. The fifth one — personal cell — is what made the Sunday close possible.
Gap
Should have stopped trying after attempt 2 — payment link for next day was the right path earlier
By attempt 3 (switching to ACH), the deal was signaling that this payment session wasn't going to succeed. Continuing through the Stripe bank connect flow added another 8-10 minutes of friction and risked the prospect's patience. The right move around minute 24 would have been: "This doesn't want to cooperate today — let me send you a secure link you can complete on your phone tonight when the bank's being friendlier. I'll hold your founders offer." That's essentially what happened by force at minute 34; it could have happened by design at minute 24.
Proposed rule for reps: if two payment attempts fail on a call, switch to a delivered payment link with a same-day or next-day deadline. Protects the prospect's patience, preserves the rep's composure, and the success rate of a link sent after a fraught call is higher than a third attempt on the same call.
Flag
Card number read aloud on recorded call — recurring compliance pattern
Jamey read his full debit card number, expiration, CVC, and zip code out loud on a recorded Zoom multiple times across the payment attempts. Gong redacts card data from playback, but this is the same compliance flag raised on Isabella's call and others. The pattern is now recurring across the dataset — reps are not proactively steering prospects away from verbal card collection even when a direct-entry link would accomplish the same thing with less risk.
Standardize the language at the payment step: "I'll send you a secure entry link right now — you drop your card in directly so I never see the number. Faster and safer than me typing it." Makes the link the default, not the fallback. This is worth escalating to Phil as a systems change, not just a rep behavior change.
Effective
Verbal close at minute 17 — Jamey said "let's try that" with no pressure
The actual closing moment was clean. Dominique finished pricing, presented the founders offer, and Jamey said "well, let's try, yeah, let's try that, all right, let's." No objections about pricing, no pushback on the waitlist, no questions about contracts. The product demo and the limited-time founders framing did the work. Had the payment flow succeeded, this would have been a 20-minute close.
Worth naming what Dominique did well pre-close: she didn't over-sell after Jamey was visibly engaged with the demo (the weight loss Tommy example at 8:33 clearly landed), and she moved to pricing at the right moment. For low-verbal prospects, reading engagement from demo reactions rather than verbal cues is the key skill, and Dominique calibrated it correctly.
Effective
Weekend recovery via text — the rescue move that closed the deal
After the call ended with no card collected, Dominique texted Jamey a payment link with the offer extended through the weekend, and confirmed with him directly via text. The deal closed Sunday. This wouldn't have happened without (1) giving Jamey her personal cell during the call, (2) committing to a specific follow-up action before ending, and (3) following through on Saturday/Sunday when most reps would have waited until Monday.
This recovery should be a documented playbook step: any call that ends with a payment failure gets a text follow-up with extended offer and a specific deadline within 24 hours. The friction of the failed call actually creates a kind of obligation — prospects who went through 10 minutes of bank errors want the account set up too, and are genuinely relieved when the rep proactively solves it for them.

What worked
Strong — competitive win
Erin was actively evaluating Aura Reality — Lucy converted a direct competitive eval
At 6:04, Erin explicitly said: "I had been looking into something called Aura. It's a 3D imaging — they take a picture of someone's face and give you a treatment plan. This caught my eye because it's something the patient could do from their own home." This is the first documented case in the dataset of a prospect mid-evaluation of a named competitor (Aura Reality) choosing Ageless instead. The winning positioning was exactly what the Battle Card says: Aura requires the patient in-practice on 13+ camera hardware; Ageless lets the patient experience it from their own phone before ever booking. Lucy didn't bash Aura — she let the use case speak for itself. Erin closed 30 minutes later.
This is the competitive win pattern we need to replicate. When a prospect names a specific competitor they're evaluating, don't attack the competitor — anchor on the specific use case difference that matters to the prospect. For Erin, the phrase that did the work was "from their own home" — she'd already internalized it as her preference. Lucy confirmed it, demonstrated it, and let the comparison complete itself.
Strong
Word of mouth + organic social profile — matched Ageless positioning cleanly
At 9:23, Erin named the acquisition reality: "almost 100% word of mouth... some advertising in a couple of locations but we also use TikTok, Instagram, Facebook." Lucy correctly pivoted — for a practice that already has organic social content, Ageless isn't a replacement for their Meta strategy, it's a layer on top of what's already driving traffic. The pitch became "optimize what you're doing through social media" rather than "build a new acquisition engine." For word-of-mouth-dominant practices adding Ageless, this is the right framing.
Word-of-mouth-dominant practices (common for NP-owned solo/small teams) need a different pitch than practices trying to build a paid funnel. Position Ageless as a conversion layer on organic social traffic — link in bio, Stories QR codes, TikTok swipe-up. The incremental revenue comes from converting the traffic they already have, not generating new top-of-funnel.
Gap
Audio issues consumed the first 3 minutes — Lucy recovered but lost demo pacing
The call opened with Lucy unable to hear Erin and Eric. After 3 minutes of "can you hear me now" attempts, Lucy ended the meeting and rejoined, which resolved the audio. This is the first audio-tech issue documented in the dataset. Lucy handled it professionally — apologized quickly, rejoined, resumed warmly — but the pacing of the first 10 minutes was compressed because of the lost time. She recovered the call successfully but it's worth flagging that tech issues at the opening are a documented risk factor.
Before any Zoom demo, test audio in Zoom settings specifically (not just system audio). The "settings → audio → test speaker/microphone" 30-second check prevents this entire failure mode. Worth adding to the pre-call checklist that onboarding shares with reps.
Well handled
"Is their photo private? Does it go away if they don't book?"
Erin's first substantive question at 6:50: "sometimes they may want to try it and then they don't want us to see it... does that go away?" This is a patient-empathy concern — Erin wanted to respect patient privacy and not have them feel surveilled. Lucy's answer was direct: the practice has access to images via the admin dashboard, Ageless doesn't sell data to third parties, the AI may use it for model improvement but not for resale. Erin accepted and moved on.
The privacy question is worth anticipating for any NP or MD-led practice — they tend to hold patient data concerns more strongly than medspa-only operators. The clean answer: "You have access, we don't sell data, image data may improve our model but never goes public." Short and factual beats a long disclaimer.
Well handled
"Does this integrate with Practice Fusion?"
At 28:48 Erin asked about Practice Fusion integration. Lucy's answer: Kerrigan bridges most EMRs including Practice Fusion, integration happens on the backend, lead flow is automated. Lucy clarified Ageless doesn't need to integrate with the patient-facing EMR — the integration is purely for routing scored leads into the practice's downstream workflow. Erin accepted.
Practice Fusion is common for NP-owned regenerative/clinical practices. Confirm Kerrigan bridge works specifically with Practice Fusion before Erin's onboarding call — worth a heads-up to onboarding so the integration is tested, not assumed.
Effective
Erin handed payment to husband Eric mid-call — Lucy kept the close alive
At 35:07, Erin said "I'm good — Eric will help you get all that set up" and stepped out to take another call. Lucy seamlessly pivoted to Eric for payment capture. This is the practice-owner-delegates-close move — common in husband-and-wife-owned practices where the clinical owner hands financial details to the business partner. Lucy handled it smoothly. Card captured live.
For family-team practices (sisters, spouses, parent-child), the decision-maker often isn't the payment authority. Don't hold the close to the decision-maker — accept the handoff gracefully and capture payment from whoever has the card. The clinical owner has already said yes; the business partner is just executing the transaction.
Effective
$499 founders club with unlimited first month positioned cleanly — no contract anchored commitment
Lucy presented the three tiers, then layered in the weekend special: founders club access, unlimited first month, skip-the-waitlist, private WhatsApp with the advisory board, and the hoodie. "Really cute pink hoodie which I just recently got and I love it." Warm, personal pitch. Eric processed payment without hesitation.
The hoodie detail continues to work — small, personal, warm. When reps mention founders swag in their own voice ("I love it," "I just got mine"), it lands as a genuine endorsement rather than a feature bullet. Coaching note: reps who haven't received their own founders hoodie yet should flag it — having one to reference warmly is a small but real close aid.

Referral-partner call, not sale

Key moments
Profile
Why this call is different
New prospect type
First consultant / channel partner call in the dataset
Natasha is a medical spa consultant who works with practices across the country — she scales, organizes, and helps implement tools for them. She booked the Ageless demo not to buy for herself but to evaluate it for her client roster. This is a completely different call shape than any other in the War Room: the rep isn't selling to the prospect, they're recruiting a referral partner.

Nellie correctly diagnosed this in the first two minutes and pivoted the entire call structure. Rather than run standard discovery, she reframed: "Let me just show you the technology and pricing, speed it through. If you feel that it works for any of your clients, we do have a referral program." The shift from sale to partnership was immediate and clean.
This is a pattern to watch for: when a prospect explicitly identifies as a consultant, agency, or services provider, stop the standard demo flow within two minutes and reset as a partnership call. The pitch is entirely different — you're not selling the product, you're selling them on becoming an effective referrer.
Strong
Referral program economics stated explicitly
Nellie laid out the program clearly: $500 payout per Ageless referral, $1,500 per RepeatMD referral. These numbers aren't currently documented anywhere else in the sales tool and they're the primary hook for channel partners. Natasha responded: "I absolutely have a list of clients that would be interested in this."
Add the referral program to the Quick Ref tab as a named section. Every rep should know the numbers in case a consultant, marketing agency, or industry colleague surfaces in discovery. It's a different pitch with different economics and shouldn't live only in this one call.
Strong
Speaking slot opportunity surfaced and captured
Natasha mentioned unprompted that she's hosting events in San Francisco and Grass Valley, California over the next couple of months with practice owners attending, and asked if someone could come speak. Nellie handled it well — offered either virtual or in-person, explained the QR-code-on-slide mechanic used at AmSpa, and committed to a custom demo link tied to Natasha's referral account so conversions from the events can be tracked.
This is a distinct use case for the tool — event deployment via consultant networks. The QR-on-slide playbook Nellie described works equally well for any practice owner hosting a patient event, referral night, or open house. Worth documenting as a named tactic in the Acquisition tab: "Event deployment — QR code on presentation slides + custom tracking link routes attendees through the demo and tags them to the source."
Effective
Channel partner fully activated — Alexandra looped in via text
Nellie didn't try to close a sale. Instead she executed three distinct partnership moves: (1) texted Natasha her personal cell + Alexandra's (referral manager) contact, (2) committed to a custom demo link for the upcoming events, (3) opened the door for co-pitch calls where Nellie joins Natasha's client intros. The relationship is now structurally set up to generate inbound referrals without Natasha having to sell anything herself — Nellie does the selling, Natasha gets paid on closes.
This is the correct execution of channel-partner onboarding. Document as a playbook: (1) recognize consultant/agency/partner intent within 2-3 minutes, (2) skip standard discovery, (3) run abbreviated demo focused on product fit for their client base, (4) lay out referral economics explicitly, (5) activate the referral manager with contact handoff, (6) offer co-pitch support so the partner doesn't have to become an Ageless expert.
Effective
Right call to skip RepeatMD refresh when Natasha declined
Nellie offered a RepeatMD refresher; Natasha declined — "it's a little tougher of a sale with the majority of my clients, especially if they have a lot of the features in their existing EMR already." Nellie didn't push. Instead she positioned Ageless as the opening wedge: "if it does make sense for them, that just opens up the door to have that conversation."
For channel partners, lead with Ageless exclusively unless they ask about RepeatMD. The Ageless-first, RepeatMD-follow-up motion is cleaner for referral economics — one product in, upsell later. Forcing RepeatMD into a consultant's pitch when they've already found it hard to sell will kill the referral relationship before it starts.

Cross-call patterns — 27 calls analyzed
These patterns emerge from analyzing all 27 calls in the dataset. They represent what is working consistently, what is breaking consistently, and what should change in rep behavior and product. Updated as more calls are added.
What's working — repeat across calls
Consistent win
The Future Aging Self is the highest-impact demo moment — every single call
Across nearly every call in the dataset, the Future Aging Self visualization produced the most visceral, memorable prospect reaction. Craig: "let's try it out." Isabella: "Oh gosh, it's terrifying." Myleen: "That's why I'm like, this is what got me." Mari: "the visual aspect means more." Linda: "Oh wow." Stephanie audibly started to say "Oh my God, I'm—" and cut herself off. Joyce called it out unprompted as the moment she'd been dreaming about for 10-12 years. This is not just the best feature — it is the most reliable closing moment in the product. If the Future Aging Self isn't shown on every call, the team is leaving the best tool in the bag.

Implication:
Demo sequencing should be built backwards from the Future Aging Self as the emotional peak. Everything before it builds tension; this is the release. Don't show it too early and don't skip it under any circumstances.
Consistent win
The sign-up-and-pause close works — now demonstrated across 8+ calls including in-call execution
Isabella, Myleen, Brette (attempted), and Linda all had timing objections — hiring, travel, website not ready, thinking it over. In every case where Mike offered to hold the waitlist spot while giving immediate community access, the prospect's resistance dropped. This is now a proven closing tool, not a one-off accommodation. It converts a "not now" into a "yes with a delay," which is dramatically better than a lost deal.

Implication:
The waitlist hold should be a standard close option for every rep, not improvised by Mike on individual calls. Document it: "We can sign you up today, give you WhatsApp access and swag immediately, and just park your account activation until you're ready. The unlimited first month starts when you say go."

Updated pattern — now demonstrated in-call as well as in follow-up.
The lever works across the full range of timing granularity: Carrie (4-6 week website redesign delay, follow-up call), Karthik (1-week wife ramp-up, in-call execution), Jen Platinum (Saturday expedite via CTO Slack, opposite direction same lever). The move is no longer an ad-hoc accommodation; it's standard playbook. Reps who don't offer it on timing-deferral deals are leaving soft closes on the table — see the Alex/Jennifer Milan coaching doc for a specific example where this move would have produced a commitment instead of a deferral.
Consistent win
Existing patient / cross-sell pivot consistently resonates when introduced
On calls where Mike or Zack pivoted to the existing patient reactivation and cross-sell angle, prospects responded positively. Isabella (Prosper email campaigns), Myleen (office manager Donna visibly excited), Mari (wellness pivot timing), Rebekah (medical-to-cosmetic), Linda (multi-modality story). The challenge is that this pivot was missed entirely on Craig's call — the single highest-value prospect in the dataset.

Implication:
The existing patient cross-sell angle must be part of every call's discovery sequence, not introduced opportunistically. Ask it directly: "How many active patients do you have in your system, and what does re-engagement look like for patients who came in once and haven't been back?"
Consistent win
VISIA-in-practice complementary positioning — now a 3-call pattern (Dusan, Yaman, Jen Platinum)
Three prospects in the dataset already own VISIA in practice and asked some version of "how is Ageless different." In each case the winning positioning was the same:
VISIA is skin-focused, in-chair, closing tool. Ageless is full-menu, phone-based, acquisition tool before the chair.
Mike's framing on Jen Platinum's call: "VISIA is really more skin focused... you've got to get them in the chair and you have your consultation going and it is really mostly for basically half of your business." On Yaman's call: Ageless as "upstream" of VISIA. On Dusan's call: Ageless positioned around the full patient lifecycle that VISIA doesn't address.

Prospects with VISIA have real sunk cost (five-figure investment) and don't want to hear Ageless replaces it. The complementary framing ("VISIA closes, Ageless fills") preserves the investment and gives them a clear mental model for how the two tools work together. All three prospects accepted the framing immediately.

Implication:
For any VISIA-owning prospect, lead with "Ageless is the upstream phone-based acquisition layer that feeds your VISIA consultations with pre-qualified patients who already have a visual in their head." Never pitch Ageless as a VISIA replacement. VISIA Battle Card already carries this language — reps should open with it the moment VISIA surfaces in discovery.
Consistent win
Event deployment — now 6+ calls, with a named sub-pattern for fitness-studio crossover
Event-anchored deals continue to be the cleanest close structure in the dataset. The pattern: prospect has a specific upcoming event (open house, trade show, vendor booth, speaking engagement), the rep identifies it during discovery, deployment timeline gets anchored to the event date, and the close compresses accordingly. Documented across Becky (June experience station), Mina (May 6 open house), Dusan (hard-deadline live event), Natasha (SF and Grass Valley speaking slots), Shawn (Saturday pilates vendor table), and Jen Platinum (attached-hotel gym partnership).

Emerging sub-pattern — fitness-studio + medical-weight-loss crossover.
Shawn's Saturday pilates vendor appearance and Jen Platinum's new location attached to a hotel with a full gym both point to a real GTM motion: medspas with GLP-1 / peptide / body-composition offerings are partnering with fitness studios to reach younger prospects. Mike surfaced a direct parallel on Shawn's call — his wife is a Pilates instructor who runs CS at Patient Now, and a local medspa runs a similar studio-embedded program. This is not a one-off; it's an emerging acquisition channel for practices expanding into lifestyle medicine.

Implication:
In discovery, always ask about upcoming events or external partnerships over the next 30-90 days. For medical-weight-loss or peptide-adjacent practices specifically, ask about fitness studio, gym, or pilates partnerships. Anchor deployment timeline to any event the prospect names and use the event date as the close deadline. Ageless QR code on vendor materials + automated follow-up to attendees is the cleanest execution of the pattern.
What's breaking — consistent gaps
Consistent win
Closing with card on call — the preferred path, and it's working
Isabella, Myleen, and Linda all signed up with card on the call. This is the desired behavior — keeping control of the close, maintaining momentum, and not letting the deal cool between a Zoom and a follow-up payment link. Gong is configured to redact credit card data from recordings, so there is no compliance concern with verbal collection.

The payment flow is shareable on screen, making the signup feel like a guided experience rather than a form to fill out alone. If a prospect genuinely can't close on the call, sending a payment link as a follow-up is the right fallback — but getting the card on the call is the win. All three of these calls demonstrate that the product sells well enough that prospects are ready to sign before they hang up. Reps should default to attempting an on-call close before offering to send a link.
Critical gap — still unresolved
No shareable demo link — now a persistent gap across 27 calls
Craig asked to try it himself — couldn't until Monday. Isabella asked — Gong recording sent instead. Rebekah asked to try it — Zack offered but couldn't. Linda asked — Dominique found a workaround (live face demo via selfie text). Now in the latest batch: Natasha asked "is there a toy within themselves they can play with?" and Dominique confirmed "we're working on it, not up yet." Laura specifically asked for a link to show her injector between now and the Wednesday close call. This has now appeared on roughly 12+ of 27 calls across 6+ reps.

A prospect who wants to experience the product is showing buying intent. Making them wait, watch a recording, or improvise a workaround is friction at exactly the wrong moment. Every time this comes up, the rep has to improvise, and the deal loses momentum between the call and any follow-up.

Action required:
A shareable, watermarked or session-limited demo link that reps can send during or immediately after the call. This is a confirmed pattern across the full dataset and is actively costing deals or extending time-to-close. Mike has flagged it to product repeatedly — the business impact is now large enough to warrant prioritization against other roadmap items.
Consistent gap

Craig, Isabella, and Rebekah all asked directly: what percentage of people who go through the visualization actually book? Consistent gap

Action required:

Minimum: (1) correct concerns entered and verified against expected output, (2) demo environment tested in the last 24 hours, (3) backup demo (Mike's photos) ready if primary fails. For clinical prospects especially, an unrealistic visualization doesn't just underperform — it actively damages trust.

(3) Differentiate Ageless — "Ageless was built specifically with that lesson — it's month-to-month, cancel inside the product, no call required." (4) Move on — don't give it more than 90 seconds.

New prospect type
Plastic surgeons require a different objection framework — legal liability is their first instinct
Dr. Wenzel is the first plastic surgeon in the dataset and his behavior pattern is meaningfully different from every other prospect. His very first response was about liability. His questions throughout the call centered on consent documentation, who's legally responsible for AI-generated images, and whether patients can use visualizations as evidence in malpractice cases. He asked for a formal contract three separate times — not because he didn't trust the product, but because formal agreements are how surgeons operate.

The standard "no contract, cancel anytime" positioning that resonates with med spa owners actually created mild discomfort for a surgeon accustomed to formal documentation. The advisory board's consent language and disclaimer framework are the most important selling point for this vertical — they should lead the conversation, not be mentioned as an afterthought.

Implication:
The Plastic Surgery vertical tab launching in June needs a dedicated objection handling section for the legal liability concern. The framework: (1) three consent checkboxes before any imaging — patient has acknowledged it's a visualization, not a guarantee; (2) the advisory board language was written specifically for clinical liability protection; (3) the visualization actually reduces malpractice exposure by replacing the patient's undocumented mental image with a documented, collaborative expectation-setting session.

New pattern — Fozia
BDR-to-AE same-day handoff is a repeatable playbook when decision-makers are physically adjacent
Zack's Fozia call is the cleanest BDR-to-AE handoff in the dataset. In under a minute at the end of the call, he: (1) identified the real decision-maker (Aria, clinic manager), (2) confirmed Fozia's approval authority to commit, (3) booked a specific same-day time (4:30pm ET) while Fozia physically walked to Aria's office, (4) confirmed the time with the decision-maker before the first call ended. The deal isn't closed yet, but the friction between now and close is minimal — no email chain, no calendar tag, no "let me check and get back to you."

This works specifically when the current prospect and the real decision-maker are in the same physical location. The playbook doesn't transfer as cleanly to distributed teams, but for owner-operated practices where the clinic manager is in the next office, it's the fastest path from first-touch to close.

Implication:
Add to rep training as a named play. Criteria: prospect on the call is bought in but defers on operational/commercial decisions, real decision-maker is physically accessible within the hour. Execute the four-step handoff while momentum is live. Do not let the call end with "I'll introduce you to Aria by email."
New prospect type — Natasha
Channel partner / consultant calls are a distinct playbook — not a standard sales call
Natasha is the first consultant in the dataset — she advises med spas nationally and wanted to evaluate Ageless for her client roster, not buy it for herself. Nellie correctly diagnosed this within the first two minutes and pivoted the entire call structure: skipped standard discovery, ran an abbreviated demo focused on product fit for practice owners, stated referral economics directly ($500 Ageless / $1,500 RepeatMD), and activated the referral manager (Alexandra) via text mid-call.

This is a completely different call shape than any other in the War Room. The rep isn't selling to the prospect — they're recruiting a referral partner. The pitch is about product fit for the partner's clients, not for the partner themselves. The close isn't a signed contract — it's an activated referral pipeline.

Implication:
When a prospect identifies as a consultant, agency, or industry services provider within the first two minutes, stop the standard demo flow and reset as a partnership call. The sales motion is: (1) recognize the partner signal fast, (2) skip standard discovery, (3) abbreviated demo focused on their client fit, (4) state referral economics explicitly, (5) loop in the referral manager on the same call, (6) offer co-pitch support so the partner can refer without becoming an expert themselves. Document as the Channel Partner Play.
New pattern — Laura
Decision committee calls require stakeholder-specific routing during the demo
Laura's call was the first full decision-committee call in the dataset — she, her practice manager Mimi, and her operations manager Russell were all on screen. Jennifer clocked this in the first 30 seconds and adjusted delivery to route specific parts of the pitch to each stakeholder: Russell got the operations/integration details, Mimi got the implementation concerns, Laura got the clinical/liability framing. The close wasn't attempted on this call — instead Jennifer booked a specific 10-minute Wednesday noon close call with all three committed to being present.

Committee calls that try to run a standard one-stakeholder demo usually fail because each person leaves with a different set of questions. Calls that route stakeholder-specific content in real time tend to close on the follow-up because each person's concerns were heard on the first call.

Implication:
When multiple stakeholders join a call, name each person's functional interest within the first minute and explicitly route portions of the demo to each. For the close, don't attempt it on the first committee call — book a short follow-up with everyone present, and use the recap email to address each stakeholder's specific concerns so they walk into the close call already aligned. The 10-minute close-call format is a strong pattern: low commitment to agree to, typically expands into a full close once momentum is live.
New playbook — Dusan
Churn-back motion — former RepeatMD customers are a qualified Ageless pipeline
Dusan is the first documented churn-back win in the dataset. Dusan closed on the same call.

This validates a specific pipeline: former RepeatMD customers who churned for reasons that have since been addressed can be re-engaged as Ageless prospects. Ageless is a lower-friction entry product than trying to convince them to re-adopt RepeatMD directly — it resets the relationship under a new product banner rather than relitigating the past.

Implication:
Pull a list of all RepeatMD customers who churned before June 2024 for payment-processing-related reasons. These are qualified Ageless prospects. The churn-back playbook: (1) acknowledge the past issue factually without defensiveness, (2) state what changed and when with specificity, (3) pivot to Ageless as a separate product — do not re-pitch RepeatMD until the new product is closed, (4) once Ageless is live and generating volume, the RepeatMD re-adoption conversation becomes natural. Every former customer who left for a resolved reason is a re-engagement opportunity.
New acceleration pattern — Dusan
Event-deadline urgency converts to same-call close via real-time internal escalation
Dusan had a webinar three days after the call and needed Ageless live for it. The escalation was visible to Dusan — he could see Mike working on his behalf in real time. Within about 10 minutes, CTO confirmation came back and the deal closed.

This is different from the waitlist-hold close (which parks a start date into the future). The event-deadline play compresses the close instead — the prospect has a hard deadline within days, and the rep converts that urgency into an on-call commitment by escalating internally with visible action.

Implication:
When a prospect surfaces a live-event deadline within a week (webinar, launch day, conference, open house), treat it as a deal accelerator rather than an objection. The sequence: (1) confirm the event date and what Ageless needs to deliver by then, (2) open internal messaging in parallel with the conversation, (3) frame the escalation as a commitment signal, not a stall ("let me confirm with the CTO right now"), (4) return with a specific answer rather than a maybe. The visible escalation is part of the close — it demonstrates that Ageless will move mountains for the prospect, which triggers reciprocity. Document as the Event Deadline Acceleration Play.

Named pipeline — 6+ cases

The existing RepeatMD customer base has produced a clear pattern of Ageless closes across multiple acquisition paths. Three distinct acquisition paths are now named:

(1)
Admin portal discovery
— existing customer logs into RepeatMD, sees Ageless listed, clicks "demo" themselves. Arrives warm and product-led. Close rate is meaningfully higher than cold inbound.

(2)
CSM / rep proactive outreach
— rep references specific dashboard metrics in the opening (Becky's $5K/mo memberships, Rob's sustained RMD use). Signals homework done, establishes credibility instantly.

(3)
Churn-back reacquisition
— former customer returning because the reason they left has been resolved. Requires factual acknowledgment of past issue, statement of what changed, pivot to Ageless as a separate product (see Dusan's panel).

Product feature pattern — 3+ calls
Beauty score pushback is a brand-alignment concern, not a product flaw — the toggle is the answer
The beauty score has produced explicit pushback in at least three documented calls: Becky Kunkel (TOPS — "feels harsh, like giving someone a C, goes against what we do"), Laura Lune (reserved response during demo), Mina Lamb Medical (concern about clinical integrity framing). The pattern is specific: practices whose brand positioning emphasizes affirmation, empowerment, or "you're already beautiful" messaging find the scoring feature actively misaligned with their voice.

Important nuance: the feature is not universally problematic. Some practices love the scoring because it drives shareability and generates the "what's your beauty score?" conversation among friends. The feature has a real segment of fans and a real segment of detractors.

The toggle-off setting in Ageless admin is the product answer. The coaching opportunity is pre-emption — naming the toggle early for brand-sensitive practices rather than discovering the objection after it's been voiced.

Implication — rep coaching:
Read the prospect's website language before the call. If the brand leans affirming ("celebrate your natural beauty," "look like the best version of yourself," "empowerment," "confidence-first"), pre-empt the beauty score objection in discovery: "the beauty score is toggleable — some practices love the viral moment it creates, some find it doesn't match their brand voice, both approaches work. You can turn it on or off in settings." That reframes the feature as an optional tool rather than a default assumption, which protects the close for brand-sensitive practices and lets fans opt-in enthusiastically.
New capability documented
Sign-up-and-pause closing lever — commit at founders club pricing, activate later
Surfaced on Stefanie Hart's Carrie follow-up call: Ageless allows practices to sign up at the founders club $499 rate and pause activation until they're ready to deploy. Stefanie's examples from her pipeline: one practice scheduled to launch June 11, another May 11 — both paid the $499 to lock founders pricing with the 30-day unlimited clock not starting until their deployment date.

This is a closing lever that hasn't been documented anywhere else in the sales tool. It resolves the most common timing objection — "we're not ready to deploy this month because of [website redesign / staff training / vacation / upcoming event / launch]" — without requiring the prospect to wait weeks for the next promotional window. The commitment is made now at the preferred price; the activation waits until the practice is actually ready to get value.

This is different from the waitlist-hold close (which is used for prospects who want to start soon but have a specific short-term conflict like travel). The sign-up-and-pause move is for prospects with longer deployment lead times who still want the founders club economics.

Implication — playbook add:
Document sign-up-and-pause as a named closing play alongside waitlist-hold. The sequence: (1) prospect names a legitimate deployment-readiness reason for delay, (2) rep offers to lock founders pricing with card on call today, (3) activation pauses until the prospect's target date, (4) 30-day unlimited clock starts on the target date rather than the signup date. This preserves urgency ("founders pricing expires Friday") while removing the activation pressure. Add to Quick Ref under Pricing & offer section with specific example dates.

New patterns from the latest batch of calls
Critical pattern — 3+ calls
The payment flow is a systemic deal risk — not a rep behavior issue
Three of the latest batch of calls surfaced payment-related friction or risk that isn't reducible to individual rep performance. Jamey's Growing Younger call had the most dramatic version — roughly 14 of 36 minutes consumed by declined debit card, ACH redirect loops, Stripe bank-connect failure, and eventually a hit daily limit. The deal only closed because Dominique gave her personal cell and followed up via text on a Sunday. Stephanie's Keraderm call had card-read-aloud on a recorded Zoom and an Aesthetic Record integration gap that surfaced mid-demo. Isabella's earlier call had the same card-read-aloud pattern. Dwayne's Powder Room call is the clean counter-example — Amex in roughly three minutes, no friction.

The recurring issues: (1) verbal card collection on recorded calls is a compliance flag, (2) small regional banks (credit unions) have weak Stripe integration reliability, (3) when on-call payment fails twice, continuing to attempt compounds the friction instead of recovering the deal, (4) daily debit limits are a real failure mode that the current flow doesn't surface early.

Implication — rep behavior:
After two failed payment attempts on a call, default to a delivered payment link with a same-day or next-day deadline. The success rate of a link sent after a fraught call is higher than a third on-call attempt. Always offer a direct-entry link over verbal card collection.

Implication — product/process (escalate to Phil):
The on-call card-read-aloud pattern is now recurring across multiple reps. A system-level fix — e.g., a standard flow that defaults to delivered payment links — is worth prioritizing over hoping reps will stop collecting cards verbally.
Rep capability — emerging signal
Dominique's recovery playbook is a documentable sales craft
Two calls from the latest batch — Jamey's payment disaster and Linda's follow-up decline — demonstrate a specific rep capability that's worth naming: Dominique's ability to hold composure and preserve the relationship when the call goes sideways. Jamey's call would have been a lost deal for most reps; she closed it two days later via text. Linda's follow-up was a clear no, and she ended it with Linda still willing to take future calls because Dominique didn't defend the price aggressively or argue the Ella situation.

The specific moves that compose this capability: (1) validate the prospect's read before reframing, (2) never blame the prospect when systems fail, (3) offer alternatives without panicking, (4) commit to concrete next steps with specific times, (5) give personal cell to create direct access — this was the single highest-leverage move that converted Jamey's failed payment into a Sunday close. None of these are scripted plays; they're posture under pressure.

Implication:
This is worth formalizing as a named playbook — "deal recovery when things go sideways" — with Dominique's calls as the teaching examples. Worth coaching across the team because every rep will eventually have a payment failure, a surprise stakeholder, or a pricing blowback. The Linda second-call tape is also the instigating case study behind the Continuation tab itself — which is a good reminder that the most valuable calls in the War Room are often the ones that didn't close.
Watch — emerging pattern
The pre-sold prospect call — short, clean, and underweighted in coaching
Dwayne's Powder Room call is 18 minutes end-to-end and closes cleanly with minimal discovery. This isn't a weak call — it's the right call shape for the prospect. The team had pre-aligned internally (Dwayne + Kelly + Scott had already discussed Ageless for days), so the rep's job was confirmation and operational walkthrough, not persuasion. Forcing more discovery would have been wheel-spinning.

The pattern is worth naming because reps default to running the same call sequence regardless of prospect warmth. A pre-sold prospect who already understands the product doesn't need the Future Aging Self full demo arc — they need (1) confirmation the branding works, (2) answer to any operational questions, (3) card on file. Jamming a full discovery sequence into a pre-sold call can actually cool the deal by introducing doubt that wasn't there.

Implication:
Add a simple read in the opening 90 seconds of any call: "Has anyone on your team already looked into this or discussed it internally?" If yes, shift to operational-walkthrough mode and close faster. The coaching point for reps is that skipping discovery on a pre-sold call isn't laziness — it's calibration. The discovery you actually need on these calls is a 3-question micro-sequence to shape implementation (services, volume, current marketing), not a full funnel dig.
Watch — emerging pattern
The low-verbal prospect requires a different discovery cadence
Jamey's call surfaced a prospect type that deserves its own handling note: the low-verbal buyer who gives monosyllabic answers throughout but is genuinely engaged. "Yeah." "Okay." "I don't know." "Yeah, we do some hormones and peptides, okay?" A rep listening for verbal affirmation of interest might bail too early. Dominique correctly read engagement from demo reactions rather than verbal cues and closed the deal.

The discovery trick for low-verbal prospects: ask yes/no or multiple-choice questions instead of open-ended ones. "Are you running paid ads or mostly word of mouth?" gets a clear short answer. "Tell me about your marketing" gets "not much." "Is it just you or do you have a front desk handling follow-up?" gets signal. "Who's on your team?" gets a shrug.

Implication:
Train reps to recognize the low-verbal pattern early (minute 2 or 3) and switch question formats. The prospect isn't disengaged — they're just not a talker. The close signal comes from demo reaction and question timing, not verbal enthusiasm.
Adjacent signal — worth tracking
RepeatMD rewards-generosity perception is surfacing in Ageless calls
Dwayne's call surfaced an RMD-specific complaint unprompted in the middle of an Ageless pitch: "our margins are going to go down to 10 percent, we're not going to make any profit." He had already had this conversation with his consultants (Lisa et al.) and had formed an opinion. Alex handled it well — clarified that rewards are à la carte and can be minimal, referenced Dr. Sona as a lean-config example — and Dwayne still closed.

This isn't an Ageless objection but it's adjacent intelligence. The RMD installed base has a rewards-generosity perception problem that's starting to bleed into sibling-product conversations. Separately, two recent Canadian RMD customers (Joyce's practice and Stephanie) have surfaced specific billing complaints — missed CSM handoffs, failed tax remittances. These are churn signals that need CS attention independent of whether Ageless closes.

Implication:
When an Ageless call surfaces an RMD complaint, don't just handle it in the moment — log it and escalate. The RMD side of the house needs to know which accounts are sounding these alarms. Longer term, the rewards-generosity perception deserves a dedicated playbook response because it's going to keep coming up.
New patterns from today's batch (Apr 21)
Named pattern — 4 cases
Existing RepeatMD → Ageless is now a named, repeatable adoption channel
The pattern has firmed up. Four documented cases now cover the full arc: Matt Ellis (admin portal self-discovery), Rob (Drive Wellness — same-day close), Becky Kunkel (TOPS — post-launch outreach, Perfect Corp comparison), and Dusan (deRMA churn-back). Close rates across this channel are substantially higher than cold inbound — these prospects arrive pre-trusting the product family and the pitch doesn't need to rebuild foundational credibility.

What the pattern teaches: the pitch motion for an existing RepeatMD customer is fundamentally different from a cold demo. Open with specific performance data from their RepeatMD dashboard (Zack's Becky opening: "$5K/mo membership revenue, that's really good"), skip foundational discovery, frame Ageless as a dashboard expansion not a new product, and lean hard on the one-dashboard answer to staff overwhelm concerns.

Implication:
Build a dedicated Existing Customer Activation script distinct from the cold demo script. Phil's team should also review admin portal placement — Matt Ellis's self-discovery pattern is the cheapest acquisition channel in the dataset. Meanwhile, existing RepeatMD accounts under $5K/mo membership revenue could be prioritized for direct Ageless outreach; they're already engaged with the platform and have the most to gain from the acquisition layer.
Named pattern — 2+ calls
Beauty score pushback is now a predictable objection for values-forward operators
Two documented cases: Becky ("beauty grade feels heartless") and Mina's softer version at Lamb Medical. Both were operator-led or values-forward prospects who reacted firmly to being assigned a number. The toggle-off feature resolved every instance in under 30 seconds, but only after the prospect had to raise the objection first.

This is no longer a niche concern — it's a predictable reaction from a specific segment (brand-forward, patient-empowerment-oriented operators). Continuing to surface the score by default and wait for objection is costing unnecessary friction on calls that would close faster.

Implication:
Move the beauty score toggle from "surface when objected" to "surface proactively during demo" for any operator-led, values-forward, or patient-centered brand. Frame language: "The score is optional — if your brand voice is 'we work with you on what YOU want,' you can turn it off in settings. A lot of practices with your brand position do exactly that." That framing validates the value before the feature answer, which closes the loop faster than waiting for pushback. Also worth flagging to product: would a default-off setting for new accounts be a reasonable choice, given how often the score produces friction?
Critical product gap
Website deployment self-serve is blocking paying customers from activating
Multiple War Room calls surfaced variants of the same deployment friction: Laura wanted to test it before deployment, Mina asked if the prospect demo link could be customized for her team, multiple prospects asked who on their side needed to "do the technical part." The Website Deployment tab in Ageless contains best practices and links but no copy-paste embed code for common website builders. The self-serve deployment flow is costing conversion-to-value for customers who have already committed.

Implication — product:
Escalate to Phil and the product team with specificity. The Website Deployment tab needs copy-paste embed code blocks for the top 5 website builders (WordPress, Squarespace, Wix, GoHighLevel, Shopify). Current state is asking the practice manager to "find a developer" at the activation moment. That's the wrong end of the funnel to introduce friction.

Implication — CX:
Every customer on their first week should have a live deployment walkthrough booked within 72 hours of signup, not a resource page handoff. The CX team's first-week success metric should be "customer has their first live Ageless link generating leads" not "customer has been onboarded."
New teaching pattern — Mandy
Cumulative rapport as the primary closing mechanism — what it actually looks like
Mandy's call closed with card on call despite Lucy dropping the Zoom for 5 minutes, zero real objection handling, and unusually light discovery. The product pitch didn't close this deal — cumulative rapport did. Mike layered five distinct rapport moves across 38 minutes: (1) correction-to-connection (sister connection at 1:02), (2) owning the prior bugging without groveling (1:22), (3) the noticed-detail compliment extended one beat (1:49), (4) using Lucy's connection drop as a rapport amplifier (21:42), (5) the spouse-humor close (33:19). Each move small on its own; cumulative effect closed the deal without requiring any of the standard closing mechanics.

The teaching value is rare. Most War Room calls document either strong objection handling or specific playbook execution. This call documents something harder to teach: what sustained, cumulative rapport actually produces when the relationship is doing the closing work invisibly. The detailed panel breakdown is in the Mandy tile.

Implication:
For reps who tend to lead with product pitch and treat rapport as a warm-up, Mandy's call should be required viewing. Rapport isn't the beginning of the call — it's the closing mechanism itself, sustained across the entire 38 minutes. The five moves are all replicable, but what matters is the compounding: each one reinforces the last.
